Iddah of Non-Menstruating Slave Woman: One and a Half Months
Ruling for slave woman who does not menstruate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the iddah of a non-menstruating slave woman and explain the proof from hadith and sahabi practice.

Core Idea
If a slave woman does not menstruate, her iddah is half of a free woman's: one and a half months.

This lesson examines the iddah of a slave woman who does not menstruate, either due to youth or old age. The Hanafi school rules that her iddah is one and a half lunar months, based on the half-iddah principle for slave women and supported by statements of Ali and Umar. We will explore the proof and the reasoning behind this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the iddah of a slave woman who does not menstruate. The ruling is wajib, and the prescribed period is one and a half months.
The source explicitly states: 'And if she does not menstruate, her iddah is one and a half months.'
2
Locate the dalil
The proof includes a hadith that a menstruating slave woman observes two menses, and athar from Ali and Umar stating the same for non-menstruating slave women.
The athar from Ali: 'The iddah of a slave woman is two menstruations; if she does not menstruate, then one and a half months.'
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The half-iddah principle is applied analogically: free women who do not menstruate observe three months (Quran 65:4), so slave women observe half that period.
The hadith on menstruating slave women establishes the half-iddah principle; the athar apply it to non-menstruating cases.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
There is no significant ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school; the ruling is considered muttafaq alayh and is supported by sahabi practice.
The source cites Ali and Umar as authorities, indicating strong early consensus.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The mufta bihi position is one and a half months, relied upon in zahir al-riwayah and later fatwa works.
The tarjih rests on the authority of the sahabi athar and the consistent half-iddah principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان كانت لا تحيض فعدتها شهر ونصف • اور اگر باندی کو حیض نہ آتا ہو تو اس کی عدت ایک ماہ اور آدھا ہے۔ او پر حدیث گزری کہ باندی کی عدت دو حیض ہیں جس سے معلوم ہوا کہ باندی کی عدت آزاد سے آدھی ہے۔ اس لئے آزاد کی عدت آیت کے اعتبار سے تین مہینے ہیں تو حیض نہ آنے پر باندی کی عدت ایک ماہ پندرہ دن ہوگی (۲) اثر میں ہے۔ عــن عــلـى قـال عـلـة الامة حيضتان فان لم تكن تحيض فشهر ونصف (سنن بیہقی ، باب عدة الامته ج سابع میں ۶۹۹ نمبر ۱۵۴۵۲) قال عمر شهر و نصف (ب) (مصنف عبدالرزاق، باب عدة الامة صغيرة او قد قعدت عن الحيض ج سابع ص ۲۲۴ نمبر ۱۲۸۸۵) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ باندی کی عدت ڈیڑھ ماہ ہے۔
And if she does not menstruate, her iddah is one and a half months. And if a slave woman does not menstruate, her iddah is one month and a half. The hadith passed earlier that the iddah of a slave woman is two menstruations, indicating that a slave woman's iddah is half of a free woman's. Therefore, since a free woman's iddah by verse is three months, so if she does not menstruate, the slave woman's iddah is one month and fifteen days. There is an athar: 'Ali said: The iddah of a slave woman is two menstruations; if she does not menstruate, then one and a half months.' 'Umar said: one month and a half. From this athar it is understood that the iddah of a slave woman is one and a half months.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It covers a slave woman who does not menstruate (due to youth or old age). Her iddah is one and a half lunar months. This is derived from the principle that a slave woman's iddah is half of a free woman's, based on the hadith about menstruating slave women. Since a free woman who does not menstruate observes three months (per Quran), a slave woman observes half: one month and fifteen days. The author supports this with athar (reports) from Ali and Umar, confirming the ruling.

Key Terms
عدة (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband before remarrying.
أمة (amah): A female slave (milk yamin).
شهر ونصف (shahr wa nisf): One and a half months (lunar calendar).

Iddah of the Free Widow: Four Months and Ten Days
Ruling for free woman whose husband dies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the iddah period for a free widow and cite the Quranic verse that establishes it.

Core Idea
The iddah of a free widow is exactly four lunar months and ten days, as explicitly commanded in the Quran.

This lesson examines the waiting period (iddah) for a free woman whose husband has died. The ruling is derived directly from the Quran and is unanimously agreed upon by all madhhabs. Understanding this ruling is essential for knowing when a widow may remarry and for ensuring legal clarity regarding paternity and inheritance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage rules on the iddah of a free widow. Determine the legal classification (hukm) of this waiting period.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites Surah al-Baqarah 2:234. Identify the type of evidence and its epistemological weight.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the verse explicitly prescribes the duration. What words indicate the exact period?
4
Note the exception for pregnancy
Recognize that the general ruling applies only if the widow is not pregnant. A pregnant widow's iddah ends at delivery.
This exception is not in the quoted verse but is derived from other texts (e.g., Quran 65:4) and consensus.

This verse explains that when a free woman's husband dies, her iddah is four months and ten days.

The author cites Surah al-Baqarah (2:234) which explicitly prescribes this period. This applies whether the woman is menstruating or not, as long as she is not pregnant. If she is pregnant, her iddah ends at delivery, which may be shorter or longer. This ruling is for free women only; slave women have half the period.

Key Terms

Iddah of the Slave Widow: Two Months and Five Days
Ruling for slave woman whose husband dies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a slave widow's iddah and identify the proof (dalil) used by the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A slave widow's iddah is half that of a free widow: two months and five days.

This lesson examines the iddah (waiting period) of a slave woman whose husband dies. While a free woman observes four months and ten days, the slave woman's iddah is exactly half. This ruling is derived through analogy and affirmed by a transmission from early scholars. Understanding this distinction is essential for accurate fiqh application in matters of marriage and inheritance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled? (Answer: iddah upon husband's death for a slave woman). What is the ruling? (Answer: two months and five days, wajib).
The ruling is clearly stated in the matn.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
What proof does the author cite? (Answer: an athar from Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib and Sulayman ibn Yasar, who were tabi'un. The text also implies qiyas on the free woman's verse.)
The athar is: 'The iddah of a slave woman when her husband dies is two months and five nights.'
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the proof establish the ruling? (Answer: The half-iddah principle: in divorce, a slave's iddah is half (two menstruations vs. three); by analogy, the death iddah is also halved. The athar confirms this.)
The principle is derived from the verse on divorce iddah and applied to death iddah via qiyas.
4
Note the consistency with Hanafi principles
The ruling follows the general Hanafi rule that a slave's iddah is half of a free woman's in both divorce and death.
No intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf exists on this ruling.

From this athar it is understood that the iddah of a slave woman upon death is two months and five days.

Transfer of Slave Woman's 'Iddah Upon Emancipation
Ruling with proof and intra-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on transfer of 'iddah when a slave woman is freed during a revocable divorce vs. irrevocable divorce or widowhood, and cite the supporting athar.

Core Idea
Emancipation changes 'iddah only if the marriage still exists at the time of freedom.

This lesson examines a nuanced Hanafi rule: whether a slave woman's waiting period changes if she is freed during her 'iddah. The answer depends on the type of separation—revocable divorce leaves the marriage intact, while irrevocable divorce or death severs it. Understanding this distinction reveals how the school links legal status to the marriage bond.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Note the two distinct cases: (1) slave freed during 'iddah of revocable divorce (raj'i), and (2) slave freed during 'iddah of irrevocable divorce (ba'in) or widowhood. The ruling differs based on whether the marriage bond is still valid at the moment of emancipation.
Revocable divorce does not dissolve the marriage immediately; the wife remains entitled to maintenance and can be taken back.
2
State the ruling for each scenario
For raj'i: the 'iddah transfers to that of a free woman (three menstrual cycles). For ba'in/widowhood: no transfer; she completes the slave's 'iddah (two cycles for divorce, two months five days for death).
The difference is the number of cycles: two vs. three.
3
Locate the proofs (athar)
The author cites two athar: from Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib regarding umm walad (showing transfer upon freedom) and from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i regarding a slave divorced twice then freed (showing no transfer if already ba'in). Identify each and note how they support the rulings.
The athar are considered mursal but accepted in the Hanafi school.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
A special case: if a woman is divorced three times (irrevocably) and then her husband dies during her 'iddah, does she observe the 'iddah of death? Abu Yusuf says no (ba'in prevents it); Abu Hanifa says yes (death revives the marriage for inheritance and 'iddah). The text favours Abu Hanifa's view.
This dispute is not about emancipation but about the effect of death after irrevocable divorce.
5
Apply the rule
Given a scenario with a slave woman, determine her 'iddah after emancipation by first identifying the type of separation. If raj'i, she becomes free and observes free 'iddah; if ba'in or widowhood, she remains on slave 'iddah.
The key is whether the marriage bond was severed before or after emancipation.

Source Text

And if a slave woman is freed during her 'iddah from a revocable divorce, her 'iddah transfers to the 'iddah of free women. And if she is freed while she is irrevocably divorced or her husband has died, her 'iddah does not transfer to the 'iddah of free women.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage states a ruling (hukm) with evidence (athar). The Hanafi position: if a slave woman is given a revocable divorce (raj'i) and then freed during her 'iddah, her 'iddah changes to that of a free woman—three menstrual cycles. This is because the marriage still exists, and emancipation elevates her status. However, if she is irrevocably divorced (ba'in) or widowed, emancipation does not change her 'iddah; she completes the slave's 'iddah (two cycles for divorce, two months five days for death). The text cites an athar from Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib regarding umm walad, and from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i. There is also an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf holds that the wife in the case of a triple divorce during fatal illness does not observe 'iddah of death, but the passage favors the view that she does both—inherits and observes 'iddah of death (this is discussed in the Urdu commentary).

Key Terms
عدة ('iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband before remarriage.
طلاق رجعى (talaq raj'i): A revocable divorce where the husband can take back his wife during the 'iddah without a new contract.
مبتوتة (mabtutah): A woman who has been irrevocably divorced (ba'in), e.g., after three divorces or khul'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A slave woman is freed during the 'iddah of an irrevocable divorce. What 'iddah does she observe?
A) Three menstrual cycles (free woman's 'iddah)
B) Two menstrual cycles (slave's 'iddah)
C) Four months ten days
D) Two months five days

Key Takeaway
Emancipation during 'iddah transfers the 'iddah to that of a free woman only if the marriage is still valid (revocable divorce); otherwise, the original slave 'iddah remains.

Reversion to Menstrual 'Iddah for Menopausal Woman
Ruling on a woman who starts her 'iddah by months due to menopause, then sees blood; her previous 'iddah is invalidated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling that a menopausal woman who begins menstruating during her month-based 'iddah must restart her 'iddah with three menstrual cycles.

Core Idea
If a menopausal woman menstruates during month-based 'iddah, the substitute falls and the original takes over.

In Hanafi fiqh, a menopausal woman observes 'iddah by three months instead of three menstrual cycles due to her lack of menses. But if, before completing those months, she begins to menstruate again, does she continue counting months or switch? The ruling, supported by an athar from al-Zuhri, is clear: the months are void, and she must start afresh with three cycles. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial situation
A menopausal woman (ayisah) is divorced and begins her 'iddah by months because she does not menstruate.
The baseline is three months for the menopausal or young pre-menstrual girl (Quran 65:4).
2
Recognize the event that triggers change
Before completing the three months, she sees blood that is considered menstrual (hayd).
This is not irregular bleeding (istihada); it must be true menstruation.
3
Apply the legal effect: invalidation of past months
The months she already observed are invalidated (mā maḍā yuʿtabaru mubṭalan). She does not count them at all.
The underlying principle: a substitute (badal) is superseded when the original becomes possible.
4
Determine the new obligation
She must restart the 'iddah from the beginning, now observing three complete menstrual cycles.
This is referred to as tasta'nif al-ʿiddah bi al-ḥayḍ.
5
Confirm the evidence
The ruling is based on an athar from al-Zuhri in which a pre-menstrual girl who observed months then menstruated was commanded to observe three cycles. The menopausal woman is analogous.
The athar is a mursal hadith, but accepted by the Hanafis because it is from a reliable tabi‘i.

And if she was a menopausal woman and observed 'iddah by months, then she saw blood, what has passed of her 'iddah is invalidated, and she must restart the 'iddah by menstruation.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage gives a ruling (hukm) with evidence. A menopausal woman (ayisah) who does not expect menses observes 'iddah by three months. If during that period, before completion, she starts menstruating, her previous months are void and she must re-commence 'iddah with three complete menstrual cycles. The reasoning: the month-based 'iddah is a substitute (badal) for the original menstrual 'iddah. When she regains the ability to menstruate, the substitute is superseded by the original. The text cites an athar from al-Zuhri where a young woman who had not menstruated observed 'iddah by months, then menstruated; he ruled she must observe three cycles. This confirms the principle.

Key Terms
آيسة (ayisah): A woman who has reached menopause and no longer menstruates due to old age.
تستأنف (tasta'nif): She starts anew / restarts from the beginning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Observe two more months (total 4 months)
C) Start over with three menstrual cycles
D) Continue with one more month then stop

Key Takeaway
If a menopausal woman menstruates during her month-based 'iddah, her counted months are void and she must restart with three menstrual cycles.

Iddah for the Menopausal Woman Who Menstruates During Months-Based Iddah
Ruling with proof from athar on iddah restart.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a menopausal woman resumes menstruation during iddah and explain why the months are invalidated.

Core Idea
Months-based iddah is a substitute; when hayd returns, the substitute is void and she must restart with three hayd.

This lesson examines a specific scenario: a woman who has reached menopause begins her iddah by counting three months, but before completing them, she menstruates. The Hanafi ruling is that her previous months are nullified and she must start a new iddah of three complete menstruations. This is based on the principle that the original ruling (hayd) takes precedence over the substitute (months) when possible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A menopausal woman begins iddah by months, then experiences menstruation before completing three months. Understanding this precise situation is key.
The woman must have started months-based iddah because she had no hayd at the time.
2
State the ruling
The months already counted are void; she must restart iddah of three complete hayd. This is the primary outcome.
The word 'tastaanif' indicates starting anew, not adding to the past.
3
Understand the rationale
Months are a substitute (badal) for hayd; when the original becomes possible, the substitute is abandoned. This is a fundamental Hanafi usul principle.
Compare to tayammum when water becomes available during salah.
4
Locate the proof
The athar from al-Zuhri: a woman who menstruated after two months must observe three hayd. This is explicit evidence.
The athar is found in Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq.
5
Apply the principle to a variant
If after restarting with hayd, she completes only one hayd and then becomes menopausal again, she would revert to months only if hayd ceases altogether. This is an extension.
Not covered in the text, but logical continuity.

And if she was menopausal and performed iddah by months then saw blood, what has passed of her iddah is nullified and she must restart the iddah by menstruation. [2091](13) And she who is married by a fasid marriage... and she who is penetrated under shubhah, their iddah is by menstruation in separation and death.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage covers two main rulings. First, a menopausal woman who begins her iddah by months (three months) but then experiences menstruation before completing her iddah: her previous months are nullified and she must restart a full iddah of three menstruations. The reasoning is that the months serve as a substitute when menstruation is absent; once she becomes capable of menstruation, the original ruling (three hayds) applies. This is supported by a narration from al-Zuhri regarding a young woman who menstruated after two months. Second, for a woman married in an invalid (fasid) marriage or who has intercourse under a misapprehension (shubhah), her iddah is always by menstruation—whether separation occurs or the man dies. She does not observe iddah of death because the relationship is not a valid marriage. This is supported by an athar from Ali ibn Abi Talib. The passage also includes a related narration from Ibrahim about a slave woman whose husband dies and she is later freed—she completes the iddah of a slave.

Key Terms
آيسة (āyisah): A woman who has reached menopause and no longer menstruates.
تستأنف (tastānif): She starts anew, from the beginning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Wait one more month to confirm the blood is genuine menstruation.

Key Takeaway
When a menopausal woman menstruates during months-based iddah, the months are void and she must restart with three hayd.

Iddah for Invalid Marriage and Shubhah Intercourse: Always by Hayd
Ruling on iddah for fasid nikah and shubhah cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a woman in an invalid marriage or with shubhah intercourse observes iddah by hayd even upon the man's death.

Core Idea
A valid marriage is required for death iddah; fasid and shubhah relationships only require hayd-based iddah.

This lesson covers the iddah for women involved in an invalid marriage contract (nikah fasid) or who have intercourse under a misapprehension (shubhah). In both cases, the Hanafi school rules that their iddah is always by menstruation, whether the separation occurs through divorce, court annulment, or the death of the man. The death iddah of four months and ten days does not apply because the relationship is not a valid marriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Fasid marriage (defective contract, e.g., missing witnesses) and shubhah intercourse (mistaken identity, e.g., intercourse thinking she is one's wife but she is not). Both require iddah due to consummation or shubhah.
The passage lumps both together for the same ruling.
2
State the common ruling
In both cases, iddah is by three hayd, and this applies whether separation (firqah) occurs or the man dies.
The text says 'fi al-firqah wa al-mawt'.
3
Understand the rationale
Death iddah is a right that comes from a valid marital bond; these relationships lack that bond. The iddah of hayd is for istibra al-rahim (purity of womb), which is still required.
Fasid marriage is not a proper nikah; shubhah has no contract at all.
4
Locate the evidence
The passage itself states the ruling; the commentary references an athar from Ali ibn Abi Talib supporting that fasid marriage iddah is by hayd. This athar is not quoted in the source_arabic but is part of the broader teaching.
The athar is found in Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah.
5
Apply to a scenario
If a man dies during a fasid marriage, the woman does not observe death iddah; she continues with hayd. If she had already started hayd-based iddah from separation, she continues that.
She may need to complete the iddah before remarrying.

آئستہ : وہ عورت جو حیض سے مایوس ہو گئی ہو اس کو پڑھاپے کی وجہ سے حیض نہ آتا ہو، تستأنف : شروع سے کرے۔
۲۰۹۱] (۱۳) جس عورت کا نکاح فاسد ہوا ہو اور شبہ میں ولی ہوئی ہو تو ان دونوں کی عدت حیض ہیں فرقت اور موت کی شکل میں۔ عورت سے نکاح فاسد کیا یا شبہ میں وطی کر لی۔ مثلا یہ سمجھ کر کہ بیوی ہے رات میں وطی کرلی بعد میں معلوم ہوا کہ وہ اجنبی ہے تو ان دونوں کے لئے تفریق ضروری ہے۔ اور تفریق کے بعد عدت گزارنی ہوگی۔ اور اگر ان دونوں کے شوہر کا انتقال ہو تب بھی عدت وفات نہیں گزارے گی بلکہ عدت تفریق یعنی تین حیض گزارے گی۔
اصل میں یہ شوہر کی بیوی ہی نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ نکاح فاسد کوحتی الامکان تو ڑ دینا چاہئے ۔ اور شبہ کی وطنی میں تو نکاح ہے ہی نہیں تو بیوی کیسے حاشیہ : (الف) حضرت ابراہیم نے فرمایا کسی باندی عورت کا شوہر مر جائے پھر آزاد کی گئی۔ فرمایا باندی کی عدت گزارتی رہے گی ۔ اور اس کے لئے باندی کی عدت کے علاوہ کچھ نہیں ہے (ب) حضرت زہری نے فرمایا جوان عورت کو طلاق دی گئی جس کو حیض نہیں آتا تھا۔ پس ایک مہینہ یا دو مہینے عدت گزاری پھر حیض آگیا۔ فرمایا اب مستقل تین حیض عدت گزارے گی۔
والموطونة بشبهة عدتهما الحيض فى الفرقة والموت
And if she was menopausal and performed iddah by months then saw blood, what has passed of her iddah is nullified and she must restart the iddah by menstruation. [2091](13) And she who is married by a fasid marriage... and she who is penetrated under shubhah, their iddah is by menstruation in separation and death.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage covers two main rulings. First, a menopausal woman who begins her iddah by months (three months) but then experiences menstruation before completing her iddah: her previous months are nullified and she must restart a full iddah of three menstruations. The reasoning is that the months serve as a substitute when menstruation is absent; once she becomes capable of menstruation, the original ruling (three hayds) applies. This is supported by a narration from al-Zuhri regarding a young woman who menstruated after two months. Second, for a woman married in an invalid (fasid) marriage or who has intercourse under a misapprehension (shubhah), her iddah is always by menstruation—whether separation occurs or the man dies. She does not observe iddah of death because the relationship is not a valid marriage. This is supported by an athar from Ali ibn Abi Talib. The passage also includes a related narration from Ibrahim about a slave woman whose husband dies and she is later freed—she completes the iddah of a slave.

Key Terms
نكاح فاسد (nikāḥ fāsid): A marriage contract that is legally invalid due to a defect in the contract, but may still produce some legal effects.
شبهة (shubhah): A situation where intercourse occurs under a misapprehension that the woman is one's wife.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a fasid marriage, if the man dies before separation, what iddah does the woman observe?
A) Death iddah of four months and ten days.
B) Iddah by menstruation (three hayd).
C) No iddah because the marriage was invalid.
D) Iddah by months if she is menopausal.

Key Takeaway
A woman from an invalid marriage or shubhah intercourse always observes iddah by hayd, regardless of how the relationship ends.

Iddah of Umm Walad: Three Menstruations Upon Master's Death or Freedom
Ruling that the iddah of an umm walad is three menstruations in both cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the iddah of an umm walad and explain the difference with the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
An umm walad's iddah is three menstruations — longer than a slave's istibra' because of her special status.

This passage addresses the iddah of an umm walad (a slave woman who bore her master a child) when her master dies or frees her. The Hanafi school holds that she must observe three menstruations, based on the practice of prominent Companions. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, considering the relationship akin to a regular slave and requiring only one menstruation (istibra'). The dispute turns on the legal status of the umm walad.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: when the master of an umm walad dies or frees her, her iddah is three menstruations. This is wajib according to Zahir al-Riwayah.
The iddah is not the same as a widow's (4 months 10 days) because the master is not a husband.
2
Identify the opposing school and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that one menstruation is sufficient, comparing the situation to istibra' for a slave woman.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i position relies on athar from Hasan al-Basri and Ibn Umar indicating one menstruation, and on the absence of a marital tie.
These athar are recorded in Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah and Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school responds by citing athar from Amr ibn al-As and Umar ibn al-Khattab, which command three menstruations. The umm walad has a special status due to her child, so her iddah is longer to ensure the womb is fully cleared.
The Hanafi position is considered mufta bihi, supported by the practice of the Companions.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference is the length of the waiting period. In a Hanafi context, an umm walad must wait three menstruations; in a Shafi'i context, she would wait only one.
This affects her ability to remarry and her rights.

Source Text

واذا مات مولی ام الولد عنها او اعتقها فعدتها ثلث حيض.

ہے۔ان عمرو بن العاص امر ام ولد اعتقت ان تعتد ثلاث حيض وكتب الى عمر فكتب بحسن رأيه (ج) (مصنف
ابن ابی هیبته ۱۵۷ ما قالوا فی ام الولد اذا اعتقت ، کم تعتد ؟ ج رابع ص ۱۵۰ نمبر ۱۸۷۵۵ر مصنف عبد الرزاق، باب عدة السرية اذا اعتقت او مات عنها سیدھاج سابع ص۲۳۲ نمبر ۱۲۹۳۱) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ ام ولد تین حیض عدت گزارے گی۔ امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ آقا ام ولد کا شوہر تو ہے نہیں اس لئے وہ استبراء کے درجے میں ہے اس لئے ایک حیض سے عدت گزارنا کافی ہے۔ اثر میں ہے۔ عن الحسن انه كان يقول عدتها حيضة اذا توفى عنها سيدها. وعن ابن عمر قال عدتها حيضة (ر) (مصنف ابن ابی هیبتة ۱۵۶ امن قال عدة ام الولد حیضته ج رابع ص ۱۵۰ نمبر ۱۸۷۴۹ / ۱۸۷۴۷ء مصنف عبدالرزاق ، باب عدة السرية اذا حاشیه : (الف) حضرت ابراہیم نے فرمایا کسی باندی عورت کا شوہر مر جائے پھر آزاد کی گئی۔ فرمایا باندی کی عدت گزارتی رہے گی ۔ اور اس کے لئے باندی کی عدت کے علاوہ کچھ نہیں ہے (ب) حضرت زہری نے فرمایا جوان عورت کو طلاق دی گئی جس کو حیض نہیں آتا تھا۔ پس ایک مہینہ یا دو مہینے عدت گزاری پھر حیض آگیا۔ فرمایا اب مستقل تین حیض عدت گزارے گی۔
And when the master of an umm walad dies or frees her, her iddah is three menstruations.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage addresses the iddah of an umm walad (a slave woman who bore a child to her master). According to the Hanafi school, when her master dies or frees her, she must observe an iddah of three menstruations. This is because she is not a wife, so the iddah of death (four months and ten days) does not apply. Instead, this iddah serves to clear the womb. The text provides supporting athar from Amr ibn al-As and Umar ibn al-Khattab. Then it presents an inter-madhhab dispute: Imam al-Shafi'i considers the relationship to be at the level of istibra' (a single period to ensure non-pregnancy), so he holds that one menstruation is sufficient. The Hanafi position, however, is that three menstruations are required, as indicated by the practice of the Companions. The commentary also includes a note that the master is not a husband, so no saddu (grief) applies, and the iddah is purely for purification.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave woman who has borne a child to her master, gaining certain rights and cannot be sold.
استبراء (istibra'): A waiting period to ensure a woman is not pregnant, typically one menstruation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how many menstruations must an umm walad observe as iddah when her master dies?
A) Four months and ten days.
B) One menstruation.
C) Three menstruations.
D) No iddah at all.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires an umm walad to observe three menstruations as iddah when her master dies or frees her, distinct from both a widow's iddah and a regular slave's istibra'.

Iddah of Umm Walad Upon Master's Death or Freedom
Ruling on umm walad's iddah with inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the iddah of an umm walad and explain the evidence and dispute with the Shafi'i school.

Core Idea
Umm walad’s iddah is three menstruations, not four months ten days, because the master is not a husband.

This lesson examines the waiting period (iddah) of an umm walad – a slave woman who bore a child to her master. When her master dies or frees her, the Hanafi school holds that her iddah should be three menstrual cycles. This stands in contrast to the iddah of a wife upon her husband’s death (four months and ten days) and also in contrast to the Shafi’i school, which considers one menstruation sufficient. We will explore the evidence from the Companions and the reasoning behind the Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi ruling is clear: upon the death of her master or upon being freed, the umm walad must observe an iddah of three complete menstrual cycles. This is based on a report from 'Amr ibn al-'As, who instructed an umm walad to do so, and was commended by 'Umar.
The iddah is for clearance of the womb (istibra'), not for mourning, since the master is not a husband.
2
Identify the opposing view
Imam al-Shafi'i and his school hold that only one menstrual cycle is required. They argue that the master is not a husband, so the umm walad is in the category of istibra', which for a slave woman upon separation or freedom is one menstruation.
They cite reports from Hasan al-Basri and Abdullah ibn 'Umar stating one menstruation.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i evidence consists of athar (reports) from the Successors: Hasan al-Basri and Ibn 'Umar explicitly said that the iddah of an umm walad upon her master's death is one menstruation.
These reports are found in Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah and Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school responds that the report from 'Amr ibn al-'As, approved by 'Umar, is stronger in terms of chain and practice. Additionally, the principle of thorough clearance of the womb (al-istibra' al-kamil) favours three cycles over one. The Hanafi view is thus preferred.
The Hanafi mufta bihi is three cycles, and the Shafi'i view is considered weak (marjuh).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence of this dispute is that in Hanafi fiqh, an umm walad must wait three menstrual cycles before remarrying, whereas in Shafi'i fiqh, she may remarry after one cycle. This affects both the timing of her marriage and the confirmation of non-pregnancy.
The difference matters in real-world cases where the umm walad wishes to remarry after her master's death.

The Sharh al-Thamiri, part three, Book of Iddah. And the woman entered upon with shubha, their iddah is menstruation in separation and death. [2092](14) And when the master of an umm walad dies or frees her, her iddah is three menstruations. (Urdu commentary: ...) Footnote: ...
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on the iddah of an umm walad (a slave woman who bore a child to her master) when her master dies or frees her. The Hanafi position is that her iddah is three menstrual cycles. The text contrasts this with the iddah of a wife, which upon husband's death is four months and ten days. The reason is that the master is not a husband, so the death iddah does not apply; however, since she had a relationship with him, her womb must be cleared, hence three menstruations. The passage then cites athar (reports) from companions to support this: 'Amr ibn al-'As instructed an umm walad to observe three menstruations, and 'Umar approved. Imam al-Shafi'i disagreed, saying that since the master is not a husband, the iddah is only one menstruation (like istibra'), citing Hasan al-Basri and Ibn 'Umar. The Hanafi response relies on the athar and the principle of thorough purification.

Key Terms
عدة (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after dissolution of marriage or similar relationship.
ام ولد (umm walad): A slave woman who bore a child to her master, thereby gaining certain rights.
حيض (hayd): Menstruation, used as a measure for iddah periods.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the iddah of an umm walad when her master dies?
A) Four months and ten days
B) Three menstrual cycles
C) One menstrual cycle
D) Until delivery

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling is three menstrual cycles based on the practice of the Companions, rejecting the Shafi'i view of one cycle.

Iddah of a Pregnant Widow of a Minor Husband
Ruling on iddah of a woman pregnant from a minor husband who dies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the iddah of a pregnant widow whose husband was a minor and explain the dispute with Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
A pregnant widow's iddah ends with delivery, even if the pregnancy is not from the minor husband.

This lesson addresses an unusual but instructive scenario: a minor boy dies leaving his wife pregnant. Although the pregnancy cannot be from him, the Hanafi school holds that her iddah is until delivery—honoring the marital bond. The Quranic verse (65:4) is applied broadly. A dispute arises from Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i, who treat her as a non-pregnant widow and assign four months and ten days. Let us examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The wife is a widow whose husband was a minor (saghir). She is pregnant, but the pregnancy is not from him. Clarify the legal context: the marriage is valid, and iddah must be observed.
Minor husband means the marriage was contracted by guardians; the pregnancy must be from a prior or subsequent relationship, but legally it is attributed to the husband for iddah purposes.
2
State the Hanafi ruling and its proof
The Hanafi ruling (Imam Abu Hanifa) is that the iddah lasts until delivery. The proof is the Quranic verse (65:4) which is general: 'And those who are pregnant, their term is when they deliver what they carry.' The Hanafi school applies this to all pregnant women, including widows.
The verse is absolute (mutlaq) and not qualified by the source of pregnancy.
3
Analyze the opposing view
Abu Yusuf (within Hanafi) and Imam al-Shafi'i hold that the iddah is four months and ten days, the standard for non-pregnant widows. Their reasoning: since the pregnancy is not from the deceased husband, the woman is effectively non-pregnant in relation to him.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf based on different interpretations of the verse's scope.
4
Understand the Hanafi response and thamarah
The Hanafi school responds that the Quranic verse is absolute and out of respect for the marital bond (hurmah al-zawjiyyah), she must observe iddah until delivery. The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf): under Hanafi, iddah ends with delivery even if it occurs immediately; under the opposing view, she must wait four months ten days even if she delivers earlier.
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) in the Hanafi school is the delivery-based iddah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واذا مات الصغير عن امرأته وبها حبل فعدتها ان تضع حملها
۲۲۰۹۳ (۱۵) اگر بچہ مرگیا بیوی چھوڑ کر اور حال یہ ہے کہ اس کو حمل ہے تو اس کی عدت وضع حمل ہے۔
یہ تو طے ہے کہ شوہر بچہ ہونے کی وجہ سے بیوی کو جو حمل ہے وہ شوہر کا نہیں ہے کسی اور کا ہے۔ لیکن چونکہ یہ بچہ شوہر ہے اس لئے اس کا احترام کرتے ہوئے بیوی کی عدت وضع حمل ہوگی۔
آیت میں حاملہ کی عدت مطلقا وضع حمل ہے۔ واولات الاحمال اجلهن ان يضعن حملهن (الف) ( آیت ۴ سورۃ الطلاق ۶۵) آیت سے معلوم ہوا کہ حاملہ کی عدت وضع حمل ہے۔
امام ابو یوسف اور امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ اس کی عدت چار ماہ دس دن ہیں۔

And if a minor dies leaving his wife and she is pregnant, her iddah is until she delivers her pregnancy. 22093 (15) If a child dies leaving his wife and she is pregnant, her iddah is delivery. It is settled that because the husband is a child, the pregnancy is not his but another's. However, since this child is the husband, out of respect for him, the wife's iddah is until delivery. The verse: 'And those who are pregnant, their term is when they deliver what they carry' (65:4). From this verse it is known that the iddah of a pregnant woman is delivery. Abu Yusuf and Imam al-Shafi'i say her iddah is four months and ten days, because this pregnancy is not the husband's, so in relation to the husband she is as if not pregnant, and the iddah of a non-pregnant woman is four months and ten days.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage deals with a case where a minor boy (who has reached puberty? The text says 'saghir' meaning minor, but he is considered a husband) dies leaving his wife who is pregnant. The Hanafi position is that her iddah is until delivery, based on the Quranic verse (65:4) which is general for all pregnant women. The text acknowledges that the pregnancy is not biologically from the minor husband, but out of respect for the marital bond, the wife observes iddah until delivery. Abu Yusuf (Hanafi) and Imam al-Shafi'i hold that the iddah is four months and ten days, because she is essentially non-pregnant in relation to him. However, the Hanafi ruling prefers delivery, citing the absolute Quranic command.

Iddah When Pregnancy Appears After Husband's Death
Ruling on iddah when pregnancy discovered after husband's death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on iddah when pregnancy is discovered after a husband's death and explain the legal reasoning from Quran 2:234.

Core Idea
Once iddah is fixed at death, subsequent pregnancy does not change it.

This passage addresses a subtle scenario: a woman becomes pregnant from another man after her husband's death, but the pregnancy is only known later. The Hanafi school holds that her iddah remains four months and ten days, the standard widow's iddah, because the obligation was fixed at the moment of death. The proof is the explicit Quranic command.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the ruling
Read the passage: a woman's husband dies, and later she discovers she is pregnant from another man. The ruling: her iddah is four months and ten days.
The pregnancy is not from the deceased husband.
2
Understand the legal reason
At the moment of death, the iddah obligation was fixed as four months ten days because the pregnancy was unknown. Once an obligation is established, subsequent events do not change it.
Obligation is fixed at the time of death (sabab).
3
Apply the Quranic proof
The passage cites Quran 2:234: 'And those of you who die and leave wives, they shall wait for themselves four months and ten days.' This verse is general and applies regardless of pregnancy status.
The dalil is qat'i al-thubut and qat'i al-dalala.
4
Note the underlying principle
The child conceived after death is not attributed to the husband (the child's act is not his). Therefore, she is treated as non-pregnant for iddah purposes.
This prevents any claim of paternity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

جس وقت بچہ شوہر مرا اس وقت حمل کا پتہ نہیں تھا تو شرعی اعتبار سے چار ماہ دس دن عدت لازم ہوگئی۔ اب وہ لازم ہونے کے بعد تبدیلی نہیں ہوگی۔ اس لئے چار ماہ دس دن ہی عدت ہوگی (۲) یوں بھی بچہ ہونے کی وجہ سے حمل اس کا نہیں ہے اس لئے اصل میں وہ غیر حاملہ ہے۔ اس لئے چار ماہ دس دن ہی لازم ہوں گے (۲) آیت میں ہے۔ والذين يتوفون منكم ويذرون ازواجا يتربصن بانفسهن اربعة اشهر وعشرا (ب) (آیت ۲۳۴ سورة البقرة ٢)

Therefore, the iddah is four months ten days. Also, because the child is not his, she is essentially non-pregnant. So four months ten days is necessary. The verse says: 'And those of you who die and leave wives, they shall wait for themselves four months and ten days' (2:234). The issue is based on the principle that the child's act is not attributed, so she is like a non-pregnant woman.

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario where a woman becomes pregnant after her husband's death (e.g., from another man), but the pregnancy is not discovered until after the husband has died. The Hanafi ruling is that her iddah remains four months and ten days, the standard iddah for a widow. The reason is that at the moment of death, the obligation of iddah was established as four months ten days (since she was not known to be pregnant). Once an obligation is fixed, it is not changed by subsequent events. Additionally, a child conceived after death cannot be from the deceased husband, so she is treated as non-pregnant for iddah purposes. The proof is from Quran 2:234. The text notes that this is based on the principle that the child's action (being conceived) is not attributed to the husband.

Key Terms
حمل (haml): Pregnancy, specifically conceived after the husband's death.
عدت (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after the death of her husband or divorce before remarrying.

Divorce During Menstruation: Counting the Iddah
Ruling that a divorce during menses does not count that menses in iddah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on counting iddah when divorce occurs during menstruation and apply it using the Quranic proof.

Core Idea
A divorce during menses is valid, but that menses does not count toward the three required iddah cycles.

This lesson examines a specific scenario: a man divorces his wife while she is menstruating. Although such a divorce is sinful (since the Prophet forbade it), it is legally effective. The critical question is whether that menstrual cycle counts towards the wife's iddah. The Hanafi school, relying on the Quranic verse requiring three quru', rules that it does not. You will learn the reasoning and how to correctly calculate the iddah in this situation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the act is a talaq given during the wife's menstruation. The ruling: the divorce is valid (though sinful), but the menstrual cycle in which it occurs is not counted in the iddah.
The prophetic prohibition (sunnah) does not affect the legal validity of the divorce.
2
Understand the exception
Normally, iddah for a divorced woman is three menstrual cycles. Here, the cycle in which divorce is given is excluded. The woman must wait three full cycles after that cycle ends.
3
Locate the dalil
The proof is from Quran 2:228: 'And divorced women shall wait for themselves three quru'.' The Hanafi school defines qur' as menstruation.
The verse is mutawatir (qati al-thubut) and its meaning is clear (qati al-dalala) regarding the number three.
4
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The verse explicitly mandates three quru'. If the divorce cycle were counted, the iddah would be only two and a half cycles (since the divorce occurred mid-cycle). This contradicts the explicit requirement of three complete cycles.
The word 'thalathah' (three) is explicit and cannot be reduced.
5
Apply the ruling practically
In practice, the woman completes the remaining part of the current cycle, then observes three full subsequent menstrual cycles. Only after completing the third cycle is her iddah complete.
If she is pregnant, iddah is until delivery, not based on cycles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۰۹۵] (۱۷) اگر مرد نے بیوی کو حیض کی حالت میں طلاق دی تو وہ حیض شمار نہیں ہوگا جس میں طلاق دی۔
حیض کی حالت میں طلاق نہیں دینا چاہئے لیکن اگر کسی نے دیدی تو وہ حیض عدت میں شمار نہیں ہوگا۔ بلکہ اگلے تین حیض عدت گزارے۔
(۱) اگر اس حیض کو شمار کریں تو عدت ڈھائی حیض ہوں گے۔ مکمل تین حیض نہیں ہوں گے جبکہ آیت میں تین کی تاکید ہے۔ والمطلقات يتربصن بانفسهن ثلثة قروء (ب) (آیت ۲۲۸ سورة البقرة ٢)
[2095] (17) If a man divorces his wife during menstruation, that menstruation in which he divorced will not be counted. It is not permissible to divorce during menstruation, but if someone does, that menstruation will not count in the iddah. Rather, she will observe three subsequent menstrual cycles. (1) If that menstruation were counted, the iddah would be two and a half cycles, not complete three, whereas the verse emphasizes three: 'And divorced women shall wait for themselves three quru' (2:228).
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling on a divorce pronounced during the wife's menstruation. While such a divorce is sinful (since the Prophet forbade it), it is still effective, but the menstrual cycle in which it is given does not count towards the iddah. The woman must complete three full menstrual cycles after that cycle ends. The reason is that if it were counted, the iddah would be only two and a half cycles, whereas the Quran explicitly requires three quru' (menstrual purity periods). The proof is from Surah al-Baqarah 2:228.

Key Terms
قرء (qur'): A menstrual cycle (or purity period); the Hanafi definition is the menstrual period itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Two and a half cycles
B) Three cycles excluding the one in which divorce occurred
C) Three cycles including the one in which divorce occurred
D) Four cycles

Key Takeaway
A divorce given during menstruation is valid, but that menstrual cycle does not count toward the obligatory three cycles of iddah, based on the explicit requirement of three complete quru' in Quran 2:228.

Iddah of a Pregnant Widow When Husband Is a Minor
Ruling on iddah of a pregnant widow when husband is a minor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the iddah of a pregnant widow whose husband was a minor, explain the Quranic evidence, and evaluate the opposing view of Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
A pregnant widow's iddah ends with delivery, even if the pregnancy is not from the deceased husband — the Quranic text is absolute.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from Hanafi fiqh: the iddah of a woman whose minor husband dies while she is pregnant. The core question is whether her iddah is four months and ten days (the general widow's iddah) or until she delivers her baby. The Hanafi school, based on a clear Quranic text, rules that delivery ends the iddah, regardless of whether the pregnancy is attributed to the deceased husband. We will analyze the evidence and the dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? The iddah of a woman whose minor husband dies while she is pregnant. The ruling is that her iddah ends with delivery.
This is the primary ruling from the matn.
2
Locate the dalil
The evidence is the Quranic verse 65:4, which states that the term of pregnant women is delivery.
The verse is quoted in the commentary.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'ajaluhunna an yada'na hamlahunna' is explicit and unconditional. It does not condition the delivery on paternity.
This is the key point in the Hanafi argument.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam Abu Yusuf (within the Hanafi school) and Imam al-Shafi'i hold that the iddah is four months and ten days because the pregnancy is not from the deceased husband.
Their evidence is the general widow's iddah verse (2:234).
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is upheld as the mufta bihi because the Quranic text is clear and does not require paternity as a condition.
The commentator supports this view.

The Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is that she completes the iddah with delivery. This is found in the zahir al-riwayah.

عدة (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of her husband before she can remarry.
صغير (saghir): A minor boy who has not reached puberty, but marriage is valid if contracted by guardians.

Second 'Iddah for Shubhah Intercourse with Interlock
Ruling and proof for second 'iddah due to shubhah intercourse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on 'iddah for shubhah intercourse during an existing 'iddah and explain the interlock principle using the proofs from 'Ali and 'Umar.

Core Idea
Shubhah intercourse during 'iddah requires a second 'iddah that interlocks with the first.

What happens when a woman already observing 'iddah from a divorce is involved in intercourse under a mistaken belief? Does she begin a new waiting period from scratch? In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling that such a situation triggers a second 'iddah, but one that runs concurrently with the first, allowing the same menstrual periods to satisfy both.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is shubhah (mistaken) intercourse during a woman's 'iddah from a ba'in divorce. The ruling is that she must observe a second 'iddah of three menstrual cycles that interlocks with the remainder of the first 'iddah.
The interlock means the same cycles count for both 'iddahs.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The proof is from the practice of 'Ali ibn Abi Talib (athar). He was brought a woman who married during her 'iddah and consummated; he ordered her to complete the first 'iddah then observe a separate 'iddah from the second marriage.
This athar is recorded in Musannaf 'Abd al-Razzaq.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'then observe a separate 'iddah' (ثم تعتد من هذا عدة مستقبلة) indicates a new obligation. The interlock is derived from the saying of 'Umar and al-Sha'bi, which specify that she observes 'iddah from both together.
The interlock is not explicit in the 'Ali narration but is clarified by 'Umar's ruling.
4
Understand the interlock principle
The two 'iddahs interlock (تداخل) meaning that the same menstrual periods fulfill both obligations. The woman does not wait additional time beyond what the longer of the two 'iddahs would require.
In the example given, one cycle already completed from first 'iddah; after shubhah, she needs three cycles total, but only two remain from first, so the second 'iddah covers the remaining two and the third completes it.
5
Summarize the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that shubhah intercourse during 'iddah makes a second 'iddah wajib and that the two 'iddahs interlock. This is based on the athar of 'Ali and the practice of 'Umar.
This ruling is from the Zahir al-Riwayah and is mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۰۹۶] (۱۸) اگر عدت گزار نے والی عورت سے شبہ میں ولی کر لی گئی تو اس پر دوسری عدت ہے۔
تشر شوہر نے بیوی کو طلاق بائنہ دی تھی جس کی وجہ سے وہ عدت گزار رہی تھی مثلا ایک حیض گزار چکی تھی کہ شوہر نے شبہ میں وطی کر لی تو اب اس عورت کو ولی بالشبہ کی عدت تین حیض گزارنی ہوگی۔ البتہ اس تین حیض گزارنے میں پہلی عدت کے بھی دو حیض گزر جائیں گے اور دونوں عد میں تداخل ہو جائیں گی۔ ولی بالشبہ کی عدت گزارنے کی دلیل یہ ہے۔ ان علی ابن ابی طالب اتى بامرأة نكحت في عدتها وبنى بها فرق بينهما امرها ان تعتد بما بقى من عدتها الاولى ثم تعتد من هذا عدة مستقبلة (ج) (مصنف عبدالرزاق ، باب نکاحھانی عدتھاج سادس ص ۲۰۸ نمبر ۱۰۵۳۲) اس اثر میں ثم تعتد من هذا عدة مستقبلہ سے فرمایا کہ وطی بالشبہ کی عدت پہلی عدت کے بعد گزارے۔ اور دونوں عدتیں تداخل ہو جائیں گی اس کی دلیل حضرت عمر کا قول ہے۔ ان عمر بن الخطاب جعل للذي تزوجت في عدتها مهرها كاملا
بما استحق منها ويفرق بينهما ولا يتناكحان ابدا وتعتد منهما جميعا ۔ اور دوسری روایت میں ہے۔ وقال الشعبي تعتد من
البيقي،
الآخر ثم تعتد بقية عدتها منها (و) (مصنف عبدالرزاق، باب نکا تھا فی عد تھاج سادس ص ۲۱۱ / ۲۱۲ نمبر ۰۵۴۵/۱۰۵۴۴ ارسنی للیتی ، باب اجتماع العد تین ج سابع ص ۷۲۵، نمبر ۱۵۵۳۹) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ دونوں عد میں تداخل ہو جائیں گی۔
(18) If a woman observing 'iddah is approached in shubhah (suspicion), then upon her is another 'iddah. Explanation: A husband gave his wife a ba'in divorce, due to which she was observing 'iddah. For example, she had completed one menstruation, then the husband approached her in shubhah. So now that woman must observe an 'iddah of three menstruations for the shubhah intercourse. However, in observing these three menstruations, the remaining two menstruations of the first 'iddah will pass, and the two 'iddahs will interlock. The proof for observing 'iddah for shubhah intercourse is that Ali ibn Abi Talib was brought a woman who married during her 'iddah and consummated the marriage; he separated them and ordered her to observe the remainder of the first 'iddah, then observe a separate 'iddah from this (second marriage). (Narrated by 'Abd al-Razzaq). In this narration, 'then observe a separate 'iddah from this' indicates that the 'iddah for shubhah intercourse is observed after the first 'iddah. And the two 'iddahs interlock. The proof for that is the saying of Umar ibn al-Khattab: He made the full mahr for the one who married during her 'iddah due to what she became entitled to, and separated them, and they shall never marry each other, and she observes 'iddah from both together. And another narration: al-Sha'bi said: She observes 'iddah from the second, then observes the remainder of her 'iddah from the first. From this narration it is known that the two 'iddahs interlock.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It states that if a woman who is already in 'iddah from a divorce is involved in sexual intercourse under shubhah (i.e., mistaken belief that it is permissible), she must observe a separate 'iddah for that intercourse. The Hanafi ruling is that the two 'iddahs interlock; that is, the same menstrual periods count for both. The proof is from the practice of 'Ali and 'Umar. The Hanafi position is that the 'iddah for shubhah is three menstrual cycles, and it runs concurrently with the previous 'iddah.

Key Terms
عدت ('iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband before remarrying.
شبہ (shubhah): Intercourse under a mistaken belief that it is permissible, e.g., believing the 'iddah has ended.
تداخل (tadaakhul): The interlocking of two 'iddahs, where the same menstrual periods count for both.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No additional 'iddah is required.
B) She must observe a separate 'iddah that interlocks with the first.
C) She must observe a separate 'iddah after completing the first.
D) She must repeat the entire first 'iddah.

Key Takeaway
A woman in 'iddah who has shubhah intercourse must observe a second 'iddah that interlocks with the first, meaning the same menstrual cycles serve both.

Interlocking of Two 'Iddahs (Tadakhul al-'Iddatayn)
Ruling on interlocking 'iddahs with supporting athar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule of interlocking 'iddahs and apply it to scenarios where two 'iddahs coincide.

Core Idea
When two waiting periods overlap, one menstrual period counts for both — 'iddahs run concurrently, not sequentially.

In Hanafi fiqh, when a woman is obligated to observe two different 'iddah periods at the same time, they interlock. This means that one menstrual cycle counts toward both waiting periods simultaneously. This rule prevents prolonging the woman's waiting period unnecessarily. The following passage from al-Sharh al-Saghir explains the ruling and provides evidence from the practice of the Companions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The passage states that two 'iddahs interlock (tadakhul). This means they run concurrently.
The key term is tadakhul.
2
Examine the counting method
Any menstruation seen counts toward both 'iddahs simultaneously. If one 'iddah ends, the remaining must still be completed.
Concurrent counting, not sequential.
3
Locate the supporting evidence
The footnote cites athar from Ibn Umar, Ali, and Umar, as well as a view from Madinan jurists and al-Sha'bi, all supporting interlock.
These are dalil naqli.
4
Understand the practical consequence
If the first 'iddah ends but the second is incomplete, she continues only for the remainder of the second.
Avoids double counting after completion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۰۹۷] (۱۹) اور دونوں عدتیں متداخل ہوں گی ، پس جو دیکھے گی حیض میں سے تو دونوں میں شمار ہوں گے۔ اور جب پوری ہو جائے گی پہلی عدت اور نہ پوری ہو دوسری تو اس پر دوسری عدت کو پورا کرنا ہے۔
حاشیہ : (الف) طلاق والی عورتیں اپنے آپ کو روکے رکھیں تین حیض (ب) حضرت ابن عمر فرماتے ہیں اگر بیوی کو حیض کی حالت میں طلاق دی تو یہ حیض شمار نہیں کیا جائے گا۔ مدینہ کے فقہاء فرمایا کرتے تھے جس نے بیوی کو حیض کی حالت میں طلاق دی یا وہ نفسا تھی تو اس پر تین حیض اس خون کے علاوہ جس میں وہ تھی یعنی الگ سے تین حیض گزار نا ہوگا۔ (ج) حضرت علی کے پاس ایک عورت لائی گئی جن سے اس کی عدت میں نکاح کیا گیا تھا اور اس کی رخصتی بھی ہوئی تھی ۔ پس دونوں میں تفریق کی گئی اور اس کو حکم دیا کہ پہلی عدت کی ما بقیہ عدت گزارے پھر یہ اگلی عدت گزارے (د) جس نے عدت میں شادی کی حضرت عمر نے اس کے لئے پورا مہر متعین کیا اس کی وجہ سے کہ وہ مستحق ہوئی۔ اور دونوں کو علیحدہ کیا۔ اور دونوں کبھی آپس میں نکاح نہ کریں، اور دونوں کی عدت ایک ساتھ گزارے۔ اور شعمی نے فرمایا پہلے دوسرے کی عدت گزارے پھر اس کی پہلی عدت کا بقیہ گزارے۔
تداخلت العدتان فيكون ما تراه من الحيض محتسبا منهما جميعا واذا انقضت العدة الاولى ولم تكمل الثانية فعليها اتمام العدة الثانية
[2097] (19) And the two 'iddahs interlock, so whatever menstruation she sees will be counted for both. And when the first 'iddah is complete but the second is not, then she must complete the second 'iddah.
Footnote: (الف) Divorced women shall keep themselves waiting for three menstrual periods. (ب) Ibn Umar says that if a woman is divorced while menstruating, that menstruation is not counted. The jurists of Madinah used to say that if a man divorces his wife while she is menstruating or in postpartum bleeding, then she must observe three menstrual periods besides that blood. (ج) A woman was brought to Ali who had married during her 'iddah and consummated, so he separated them and ordered her to complete the remainder of the first 'iddah, then observe another 'iddah. (د) For one who married during 'iddah, Umar fixed a full mahr because she became entitled to it, separated them, they shall never marry, and she observes 'iddah from both together. And al-Sha'bi said: She observes 'iddah from the second, then completes the first.
The two 'iddahs interlock, so whatever menstruation she sees counts for both. If the first 'iddah ends but the second is not complete, she must complete the second.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage clarifies the Hanafi rule of interlocking 'iddahs. When two waiting periods coincide (e.g., from divorce and from shubhah), they run concurrently; one menstrual period serves both. If the first 'iddah is completed before the second, she must continue for the remainder of the second. The footnote provides supporting athar from Ibn Umar, Ali, and Umar, as well as a view of Madinan jurists and al-Sha'bi, all confirming interlock.

Key Terms
تداخل (tadakhul): The merging of two 'iddahs so that one menstrual period counts for both.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) She must start the second 'iddah from the beginning.
D) She does nothing, as the first 'iddah suffices.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that concurrent 'iddahs count simultaneously to avoid unnecessary prolongation, supported by companion practice.

When Does 'Iddah Begin? The Hanafi Rule of Immediate Commencement
Ruling on when 'iddah begins and effect of ignorance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the commencement of 'iddah after divorce and death, and apply it to cases where the woman is unaware.

Core Idea
The 'iddah begins at the moment of divorce or death, not when she learns.

A common question is whether a woman must observe 'iddah from the day she finds out about her divorce or husband's death, or from the actual event. The Hanafi school has a clear position based on the practice of the Companions. Let's explore the ruling and its proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the commencement of 'iddah. The ruling is that it begins immediately after divorce (talaq) or death (wafat).
The 'iddah period itself is wajib; this rule determines when to start counting.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites an athar from Ibn Umar (radiyallahu anhu) recorded in Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah.
The athar is a qawl sahabi, which Hanafis accept as a proof when no other evidence contradicts.
3
Understand the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit wording 'min yawmi tallaqaha' (from the day he divorced her) indicates that the start is tied to the event itself, not to knowledge.
The phrase 'min yawmi' is clear and general, covering all cases.
4
Apply to the case of ignorance
If the full 'iddah period passes without her knowledge, it is complete. She does not need to repeat it.
The cause (sabab) has occurred, so the effect ('iddah) runs automatically.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۰۹۸](۲۰) وابتداء العدة في الطلاق عقيب الطلاق وفى الوفاة عقيب الوفاة فان لم تعلم بالطلاق او الوفاة حتى مضت مدة العدة فقد انقضت عدتها
عدت کی ابتدا طلاق میں طلاق کے بعد سے ہوگی اور وفات میں وفات کے بعد سے ہوگی ، پس اگر علم نہ ہو اس کو طلاق کا یا وفات کا یہاں تک کہ عدت کی مدت گزرگئی تو اس کی عدت پوری ہو گئی۔
اثر میں ہے۔ عن ابن عمر قال عدتها من يوم طلقها ومن يوم يموت عنها (الف) (مصنف ابن ابی هیته ۱۸۲ما قالوافي المرأة يطلقها زوجها ثم بموت عنها من ای یوم تعتد ؟ ج رابع ، ص ۱۶۶ نمبر ۱۸۹۱۰) اس اثر میں ہے کہ طلاق کے بعد اور وفات کے بعد عدت گزرنی شروع ہو جائے گی چاہے عورت کو طلاق اور وفات کا علم ہو یا نہ ہو۔ چنانچہ تین حیض کے بعد اس کو طلاق کا علم ہوا یا چار ماہ دس روز کے بعد شوہر کے مرنے کا علم ہوا تو عدت گزر چکی ہوگی (۲) عدت کے اسباب طلاق اور وفات ہیں اس لئے یہ دونوں ہو تو عدت شروع ہو جائے گی کیونکہ سبب پایا گیا۔
[2098] (20) The beginning of 'iddah in divorce is immediately after divorce, and in death immediately after death. If she did not know of the divorce or death until the period of 'iddah passed, then her 'iddah has expired. ... 'Iddah in divorce begins after divorce, and in death after death. So if she did not know of the divorce or death until the 'iddah period passed, then her 'iddah is complete. There is an athar: from Ibn Umar, he said: Her 'iddah is from the day he divorced her and from the day he dies. (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, no. 18910) This athar indicates that 'iddah begins after divorce and death, whether the woman knows or not. Thus, if she learns of the divorce after three menstruations, or of the death after four months ten days, her 'iddah is already complete. The causes of 'iddah are divorce and death, so when these occur, 'iddah begins because the cause has been realized.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage states a clear Hanafi ruling: 'iddah commences immediately upon the occurrence of the cause—divorce or death—regardless of the woman's knowledge. If the full period passes without her knowledge, her 'iddah is complete. The proof is the athar of Ibn Umar and the principle that causes take effect upon occurrence.

Key Terms
ابتداء عدة (ibtida' al-'iddah): The moment from which the 'iddah period begins to count.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) She must observe 'iddah for another four months and ten days from learning.
D) She does not need to observe 'iddah because she was unaware.

Key Takeaway
The 'iddah begins at the event itself (divorce or death); knowledge is not required for commencement.

Overlapping 'Iddahs (تداخل العدد)
Explains when two 'iddahs coincide and how to complete them

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on overlapping 'iddahs and apply the principle of tadakhul to determine the total waiting period when two 'iddahs coincide.

Core Idea
When two 'iddahs overlap, the same menstrual periods count for both, avoiding prolonged waiting.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi principle of تداخل العدد — overlapping of waiting periods. When a woman finds herself under two simultaneous 'iddahs, the question arises: must she observe them consecutively, or can they run concurrently? The Hanafi school, based on a statement of Ali (RA), allows the woman's menstrual cycles to serve both 'iddahs simultaneously, with the condition that if the first ends before the second, she must complete the remaining period. Let us unpack the ruling and its practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two 'iddahs
Recognize that the scenario involves two separate causes of 'iddah (e.g., divorce and dubius intercourse) that begin at different times.
2
Apply tadakhul
Once overlapping begins, each menstrual period counts for both 'iddahs simultaneously.
This is the core principle: concurrent counting.
3
Determine the endpoint
If the first 'iddah ends before the second is complete, the woman must continue observing the second 'iddah until its full term.
She cannot stop after the first.
4
Work through the example
Trace the given example: after one menstruation, dubius intercourse occurs. The remaining two periods count for both, then one extra period completes the second 'iddah. Total: 4 periods from divorce, 3 from dubia.
The longer 'iddah determines total waiting time.
5
Review the evidence
The Hanafi proof is the qawl of Ali (RA) recorded in Musannaf 'Abd al-Razzaq, indicating that separate 'iddahs are observed sequentially, but tadakhul applies when possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تداخلت العدتان فيكون ما تراه من الحيض محتسبا منهما جميعا واذا انقضت العدة الاولى ولم تكمل الثانية فعليها اتمام العدة الثانية. دوعد میں جمع ہو جائیں، ایک عدت طلاق کی اور دوسری عدت وطی بالشبہ کی توجب طلاق کی عدت گزر جائے گی تو اس کے اندر وطی بالشبہ کی بھی عدت گزر جائے گی ۔ مثلا مثال مذکور میں ایک حیض گزرنے کے بعد ولی بالشبہ ہوئی تو طلاق کی عدت دو حیض اور گزارنا ہے۔ اس لئے اس کے اندر دو حیض ولی بالشبہ کے بھی گزر جائیں گے اور ایک حیض مزید ولی بالشبہ کا گزارے۔ جس سے تین حیض پورے ہو جائیں گے۔ حضرت علی کا قول پہلے گزر چکا ہے ثم تعتد من هذا عدة مستقبلة (مصنف عبدالرزاق نمبر ۱۰۵۳۲)
The two 'iddahs overlap, so what she sees of menstruation will be counted from both of them. If the first 'iddah expires and the second is not yet complete, she must complete the second 'iddah. If two 'iddas combine, one 'iddah of divorce and the second 'iddah of dubius intercourse, then when the 'iddah of divorce passes, within it the 'iddah of dubius intercourse also passes. For example: in the mentioned example, after one menstruation passed, dubius intercourse occurred, so the 'iddah of divorce requires two more menstruations. Therefore within them two menstruations for the dubius intercourse also pass, and one additional menstruation for the dubius intercourse passes, completing three menstruations. The saying of Ali (RA) has passed previously: then she must observe a separate 'iddah for this (Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq no. 10532).
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage states the ruling on overlapping 'iddahs. It clarifies that if a woman is undergoing two 'iddahs simultaneously (e.g., one from divorce and one from dubious intercourse), the menstrual periods she experiences may serve to fulfill both 'iddahs. If the first 'iddah ends while the second is incomplete, she must continue until the second is fully observed. The Hanafi position, based on a report from Ali (RA), uses the principle of 'تداخل العدد' (overlapping of counts). The Urdu commentary illustrates with an example: a woman divorced and then had dubious intercourse; if one period passed before the dubia, the remaining two periods count for both 'iddahs, and an additional period completes the three. This avoids unnecessary prolongation of the waiting period.

Key Terms
تداخل العدد (tadākhul al-‘idad): The legal principle that two or more waiting periods may overlap and be fulfilled simultaneously by the same menstrual cycles or time periods.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling on overlapping 'iddahs, if a woman's divorce 'iddah ends before her dubius intercourse 'iddah, what must she do?
A) She is free to remarry immediately
B) She must complete the remaining 'iddah for the dubius intercourse
C) She must start a new 'iddah for the dubius intercourse from the beginning
D) She must observe a separate 'iddah for each cause consecutively

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle of tadakhul al-'idad allows overlapping 'iddahs to run concurrently, with the woman completing any remaining period of the longer 'iddah.

Commencement of 'Iddah in a Fāsid Marriage
Ruling: 'iddah triggers in fāsid marriage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when 'iddah begins in a fāsid marriage and explain the reasoning (qiyas) behind it.

Core Idea
'Iddah for a fāsid marriage starts upon separation or firm resolve to stop intercourse.

This lesson addresses a specific scenario: a marriage that is defective (fāsid) but not void. Unlike a valid marriage where 'iddah begins after divorce, here the trigger is the effective end of the marital relationship—either by a judge's order or the husband's definitive intention. Understanding this nuance is essential for correctly applying 'iddah rules in irregular unions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Read the passage and note the two triggers for 'iddah: judicial separation or the husband's firm resolve to cease intercourse.
The ruling distinguishes between external (qāḍī) and internal (husband's intent) termination.
2
Understand the underlying reasoning
Recognize that the 'illa (effective cause) is the definitive end of conjugal rights. In a fāsid marriage, divorce is not needed; separation itself suffices.
This is based on qiyas jali: the same cause produces the same effect.
3
Contrast with valid marriage
Note that in a valid marriage, 'iddah follows divorce; here it follows separation or firm resolve, because the contract is defective.
The absence of a valid contract eliminates the need for formal divorce.
4
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Given a concrete case (e.g., husband resolves never to have intercourse), determine the start date of 'iddah.
The moment of firm resolve is the key moment.

والعدة فى النكاح الفاسد عقيب التفريق بينهما او عزم الواطى على ترك وطيها. اور عدت نکاح فاسد میں دونوں کے درمیان تفریق کے بعد یا ولی کرنے والے نے وطی چھوڑنے کے پختہ ارادہ کے بعد ۔ نکاح فاسد کیا ہو تو وہ صحیح نکاح نہیں ہے اس لئے تفریق کرانا ہی طلاق کے درجے میں ہے۔ اس لئے تفریق کے بعد ہی عدت شروع ہو جائے گی۔ یا شوہر پختہ ارادہ کرے کہ آج تاریخ سے اس عورت سے ولی نہیں کرنا ہے تو جس تاریخ سے وطی نہ کرنے کا پختہ ارادہ کر لیا اس تاریخ سے عدت شروع ہو جائے گی۔ کیونکہ نکاح تو صحیح ہے نہیں کہ طلاق دینے کی ضرورت پڑے۔ اس لئے وطی نہ کرنے کا پختہ ارادہ کرنا تفریق کا شائبہ ہے۔ اس لئے پختہ ارادہ کے بعد عدت شروع ہو جائے گی۔ فرق اتنا ہے کہ پہلے قاضی نے تفریق کرائی اور اب یہ خود تفریق کی طرف قدم اٹھا رہا ہے۔
And the 'iddah for an invalid marriage is after separation between them or after the one who had intercourse firmly resolves to leave intercourse with her. And the 'iddah of invalid marriage begins after separation between them or after the one who committed intercourse firmly resolves to stop intercourse. An invalid marriage is not a valid marriage, so effecting separation is at the level of divorce. Therefore 'iddah begins after separation. Or the husband firmly resolves that from today he will not have intercourse with that woman, then from the date he firmly resolved not to have intercourse, the 'iddah begins. Because the marriage is not valid so that there is need to divorce. Therefore firmly resolving not to have intercourse is like separation. So after firm resolve, 'iddah begins. The difference is that in the first, the judge effects separation, and here he himself is taking a step toward separation.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage explains when the 'iddah begins for a woman married under a fāsid (invalid) contract. The ruling: 'iddah starts after judicial separation (by a qāḍī) or when the man who had intercourse firmly resolves never to have intercourse again. The reasoning is that a fāsid marriage does not require a formal divorce; separation itself is equivalent to divorce. If the man resolves to stop intercourse, that intention is akin to initiating separation, so the 'iddah begins from that date. The Urdu commentary elaborates that if a judge orders separation, it is clear; if the man himself decides, it is as if he is moving toward separation. This is a Hanafi position based on qiyas (analogy) to the commencement of 'iddah in valid marriages, adjusted for the nature of fāsid contracts.

Key Terms
نکاح فاسد (nikāh fāsid): A marriage contract that is defective in some essential condition (e.g., without witnesses), but not fully invalid (bāṭil). Hanafi: it can be rectified and produces legal effects like 'iddah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) From the day the judge declares separation
B) From the day the husband firmly resolved not to have intercourse
C) From the day of the contract
D) There is no 'iddah for fāsid marriage

Key Takeaway
In a fāsid marriage, 'iddah begins upon judicial separation or the husband's firm resolve to cease intercourse, not upon divorce.

Mourning for the Mubtattah and Widow: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Establishes that mourning is obligatory for divorced bain and widows who are adult Muslims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on mourning (ihdād) for a woman divorced bā'in and explain both the Hanafi proof from the hadith and the Shafi'i counter-argument, as well as the practical consequence of the ikhtilaf.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school obligates mourning for both widow and irrevocably divorced woman based on the general term 'zawj' in the hadith.

This passage addresses a key difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: whether a woman who has received an irrevocable divorce (bā'in) must observe mourning (ihdād) during her 'iddah. The Hanafi school holds that she must, just like a widow, while Imam al-Shafi'i restricts mourning only to widows. Understanding this ikhtilaf requires close reading of the hadith and its linguistic interpretation.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Clearly articulate that both the mubtattah (irrevocably divorced) and the widow are required to mourn if they are adult Muslims.
The ruling applies during the 'iddah period.
2
Identify the primary dalil
Locate the hadith quoted in the passage: the Prophet (SAW) said that a woman must not mourn more than three days except for a husband (zawj), with a period of four months and ten days for a widow.
The hadith is from Umm Habiba and Umm 'Atiyyah (Bukhari and Muslim).
3
Analyze the Hanafi interpretation
The Hanafi school takes the term 'zawj' as general ('āmm), applying to both the deceased husband and the ex-husband after bā'in divorce. The duration mentioned (four months ten days) is only for widows; for the divorced woman, the period is the 'iddah.
The Hanafi principle: al-‘āmm yufīdu al-wujūb ‘alā kull afrādihī (the general term obligates all its instances).
4
Analyze the Shafi'i interpretation
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that the hadith is specific to the deceased husband because it mentions 'four months and ten days', which is the 'iddah of death, and the phrase 'except for a husband' (illā ‘alā zawj) refers only to the husband who died.
Thus, the divorced woman does not mourn.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: in the Hanafi school, a woman divorced bā'in must refrain from adornment (kohl, dyed clothes, etc.) during her 'iddah; in the Shafi'i school, she may dress normally.
This affects daily life and religious practice.
