وعلى المبتوتة والمتوفى عنها زوجها اذا كانت بالغة مسلمة الاحداد. معندہ بائنہ اور جس کا شوہر مر گیا ہو جبکہ وہ بالغہ اور مسلمیہ ہے تو سوگ منانا ہے۔ بالغہ اور مسلمہ عورت ہو اس کو طلاق بائنہ دی ہو جس کی وہ عدت گزار رہی ہو یا اس کے شوہر کا انتقال ہو گیا ہو جس کی وہ عدت گزار رہی ہو اس زمانے میں وہ سوگ منائے ۔ حدیث میں ہے۔ دخلت على ام حبيبة زوج النبي ... اني سمعت رسول الله الله يقول لا يحل لامرأة تؤمن بالله واليوم الآخر ان تحد على ميت فوق ثلاث ليال الا على زوج اربعة اشهر وعشرا (الف) ( بخاری شریف، باب تحد المتوفى عنها اربعة اشهر وعشراص ۸۰۳ نمبر ۵۳۳۴ / مسلم شریف ، باب وجوب الاحداد فى عدة الوفات وتحریمۃ فی غیر ذلک الا ثلاثۃ ایام ص ۴۸۷ نمبر ۷ ۱۴۸) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ متوفی عنھا چار ماہ دس دن سوگ منائے گی (۲) اور طلاق بائنہ دی ہوئی سوگ منائے اس کا استدلال اس حدیث سے ہو سکتا ہے جن میں مطلقا زوج کا لفظ استعمال کیا ہے چاہے وہ طلاق بائنہ والا شوہر ہو چاہے انتقال کیا ہوا شو ہر ہو۔ عن ام عطية قالت قال النبي الا الله لا يحل لامرأة تؤمن بالله واليوم الآخر ان تحد فوق ثلاث الا على زوج فانها لا تكتحل ولا تلبس ثوبا مصبوغا الا ثوب عصب (ب) ( بخاری شریف، باب تلبس الحادة ثياب العصب ص ۸۰۴ نمبر ۱۵۳۴۲ مسلم شریف باب وجوب الاحداد في عدة والوفات وتحريمة فی غیر ذلک الا ثلاثۃ ایام ص ۷ ۴۸ نمبر ۱۴۹۰) اس حدیث میں زوج کا لفظ مطلق ہے ۔ جس سے متوفی عنہ بھی ہو سکتا ہے اور معدہ یہ بھی ۔ اس لئے معدہ بائنہ بھی عدت میں سوگ منائے گی (۳) جس طرح متوفی عنھا کو شوہر کے مرنے کا افسوس ہے اسی طرح طلاق بائنہ والی کو شوہر کے چھوٹنے کا افسوس ہے اس لئے وہ بھی سوگ منائے گی۔ امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ حدیث میں حصر کے ساتھ صرف متوفی عنہا کو سوگ منانے کے لئے کہا گیا ہے اس لئے طلاق بائنہ والی کو سوگ منانے کی ضرورت نہیں ہے۔
And upon the mubtattah (irrevocably divorced woman) and the one whose husband has died, if she is an adult Muslim, is mourning. The mubtattah and the widow, if she is an adult Muslim, must observe mourning. She mourns if she is an adult Muslim woman, either divorced by a bā'in divorce and observing 'iddah, or her husband has died and she is observing 'iddah. In a hadith: I entered upon Umm Habiba, the wife of the Prophet (SAW)... I heard the Messenger of Allah (SAW) say: It is not permissible for a woman who believes in Allah and the Last Day to mourn for a dead person more than three nights, except for a husband, four months and ten days. (Bukhari, Bab Tuhidd al-Mutawaffa 'Anha Arba'at Ashhur wa 'Ashran, p. 803 no. 5334; Muslim, Bab Wujub al-Ihdad fi 'Iddat al-Wafat wa Tahrimihi fi Ghayr Dhalik illa Thalathat Ayyam, p. 487 no. 148) From this hadith it is known that the widow mourns four months and ten days. Also, the woman given bā'in divorce mourns, and the evidence for this can be taken from the hadith that uses the word زوج (husband) in an absolute sense, whether the husband divorced by bā'in or the deceased husband. From Umm 'Atiyyah (RA) said: The Prophet (SAW) said: It is not permissible for a woman who believes in Allah and the Last Day to mourn more than three (days) except for a husband, for she should not apply kohl, nor wear dyed clothes except 'usb cloth. (Bukhari, Bab Talbas al-Hadda Thiyab al-'Usb, p. 804 no. 15342; Muslim, Bab Wujub al-Ihdad fi 'Iddat al-Wafat wa Tahrimihi fi Ghayr Dhalik illa Thalathat Ayyam, p. 748 no. 1490) In this hadith the word زوج is absolute, which can include both the deceased husband and the one who divorced bā'in. Therefore the mubtattah also mourns during 'iddah. Just as the widow has grief over her husband's death, similarly the woman divorced bā'in has grief over her husband leaving her, so she also mourns. Imam al-Shafi'i says that in the hadith 'except for a husband' is restricted (only for the husband of a widow), so the mubtattah does not need to mourn.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding mourning (ihdād) for a woman divorced with a bā'in (irrevocable) divorce. The Hanafi ruling: both the mubtattah and the widow must observe mourning if they are adult Muslims. The proof is from two hadiths: one from Umm Habiba and another from Umm 'Atiyyah. The Hanafi interpretation is that the word 'zawj' in the hadith is general ('āmm) and applies to both the deceased husband and the ex-husband after a bā'in divorce. The reasoning: grief over separation is similar to grief over death. The Shafi'i position: the hadith restricts mourning to the husband in the context of death, so a divorced woman does not mourn. The Hanafi response, implicit in the text, is that the absolute wording includes both, and the analogy of grief supports it.

Key Terms
المبتوتة (al-mubtattah): A woman who has been irrevocably divorced (talāq bā'in), meaning the husband cannot take her back without a new marriage contract.
إحداد (ihdād): Mourning, which includes refraining from adornment such as kohl, perfume, and colored clothes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because the hadith applies to any husband (zawj)
B) No, because mourning is only for death
C) Only if she has children
D) Only if she was married for more than a year

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the obligation of mourning applies to both widows and women divorced bā'in, based on the general wording of the hadith.

Prohibited Acts in Mourning (Ihdād)
Lists what a mourning woman must avoid: perfume, adornment, oil, kohl, except for excuse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the items prohibited during mourning (ihdād) and apply the exception for medical necessity.

Core Idea
Perfume, adornment, oil, and kohl are forbidden during mourning except for genuine excuses like illness.

This lesson details the specific prohibitions of mourning (ihdād) for a woman whose marriage has ended by death or bā'in divorce. The Hanafi school requires abstention from items of beautification, but recognizes a narrow exception for necessity. Understanding these rules ensures that a woman fulfills her obligation while preserving health and dignity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the passage discusses 'ihdād' (mourning observances) and its requirements: leaving off specific items. The ruling is wājib (obligatory) for a widow and for a woman divorced by bā'in talaq during her 'iddah.
The prohibition applies to items used for beautification.
2
List the explicitly prohibited items
From the text: perfume (ṭīb), adornment (zīnah), oil (duhn), and kohl (kuḥl). The Urdu commentary adds that all adornment items are included.
These are categories; any item primarily used for beautification falls under them.
3
Note the exception for necessity ('udhr)
The text states 'except for an excuse' (إلا من عذر). The commentary explains that illness or medical need, such as an eye infection requiring kohl, permits usage. The exception is restricted to genuine necessity.
The exception is not a license for beautification; it only covers essential health needs.
4
Connect to the hadith evidence
The ruling is derived from the hadith of Umm 'Atiyyah (found in Bukhari and Muslim), which prohibits kohl and dyed clothes. The Hanafi school generalizes this to all adornments via the principle that what is prohibited in kind includes similar items.
The hadith is aḥād; its transmission is zannī but its meaning (dalāla) is clear and binding in practice.

And mourning is that she leaves off perfume, adornment, oil, and kohl except for an excuse. This passage details the practical obligations of mourning (ihdād). The woman must abstain from perfume (ṭīb), adornment (zīnah), oil (duhn), and kohl (kuḥl) — items that beautify. This is distinct from the Shafi'i position which may limit it to what is explicitly mentioned in the hadith.

Ihdād Obligation for Irrevocably Divorced and Widowed Women
Establishes ihdād obligation for mubtūtah and mutawaffā 'anhā with proofs and inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ihdād for the mubtūtah and the mutawaffā 'anhā, explain the dalil from hadith, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school obligates ihdād on both widows and irrevocably divorced women because the hadith's word 'zawj' is general and the grief is analogous.

This passage addresses a key disagreement between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: whether a woman given an irrevocable divorce (mubtūtah) must observe the mourning period (ihdād) just as a widow does. The Hanafi school, relying on the general word 'husband' in the hadith and an analogy of grief, rules that she must. We will examine the proofs and the opposing view.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling in the matn: both mubtūtah and mutawaffā 'anhā must observe ihdād for four months and ten days if they are adult and Muslim.
The text opens with 'Upon the irrevocably divorced woman and the one whose husband has died... ihdād is obligatory.'
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that only the widow mourns; the divorced woman does not. Understand the basis: the hadith says 'except for a husband' which they interpret as specifically the deceased husband.
The Shafi'i reading treats the exception as restrictive.
3
Follow the Shafi'i proof
Analyze the Shafi'i dalil: the hadith uses 'illa 'alā zawj' (except for a husband) which, in Arabic, can limit the exemption to the husband who died.
They also note that the period of mourning for a husband is uniquely long, implying it is for death, not divorce.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafis argue that the term 'zawj' is absolute and includes all husbands, dead or divorced. They also offer a qiyas: both types of women experience loss and grief.
The commentator adds: 'Just as a widow feels grief for death, the divorced woman feels grief for separation.'
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Hanafi law, a divorced woman must observe ihdād with all its restrictions; under Shafi'i, she does not.
This difference affects her ability to remarry, social interactions, and adornment during iddah.

معندہ بائنہ اور جس کا شوہر مر گیا ہو جبکہ وہ بالغہ اور مسلمیہ ہے تو سوگ منانا ہے۔ ت بالغہ اور مسلمہ عورت ہو اس کو طلاق بائنہ دی ہو جس کی وہ عدت گزار رہی ہو یا اس کے شوہر کا انتقال ہو گیا ہو جس کی وہ عدت گزار رہی ہو اس زمانے میں وہ سوگ منائے ۔ سوگ منانے کی تفصیل آگے آرہی ہے۔
حدیث میں ہے۔ دخلت على ام حبيبة زوج النبي ... اني سمعت رسول الله الله يقول لا يحل لامرأة تؤمن
بالله واليوم الآخر ان تحد على ميت فوق ثلاث ليال الا على زوج اربعة اشهر وعشرا (الف) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ متوفی عنھا چار ماہ دس دن سوگ منائے گی (۲) اور طلاق بائنہ دی ہوئی سوگ منائے اس کا استدلال اس حدیث سے ہو سکتا ہے جن میں مطلقا زوج کا لفظ استعمال کیا ہے چاہے وہ طلاق بائنہ والا شوہر ہو چاہے انتقال کیا ہوا شو ہر ہو۔ عن ام عطية قالت قال النبي الا الله لا يحل لامرأة تؤمن بالله واليوم الآخر ان تحد فوق ثلاث الا على زوج فانها لا تكتحل ولا تلبس ثوبا مصبوغا الا ثوب عصب (ب) اس حدیث میں زوج کا لفظ مطلق ہے ۔ جس سے متوفی عنہ بھی ہو سکتا ہے اور معدہ یہ بھی ۔ اس لئے معدہ بائنہ بھی عدت میں سوگ منائے گی (۳) جس طرح متوفی عنھا کو شوہر کے مرنے کا افسوس ہے اسی طرح طلاق بائنہ والی کو شوہر کے چھوٹنے کا افسوس ہے اس لئے وہ بھی سوگ منائے گی۔ امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ حدیث میں حصر کے ساتھ صرف متوفی عنہا کو سوگ منانے کے لئے کہا گیا ہے اس لئے طلاق بائنہ والی کو سوگ منانے کی ضرورت نہیں ہے۔
Ruling 2100 (22): Upon the irrevocably divorced woman and the one whose husband has died, if she is adult and Muslim, ihdād is obligatory. Commentary: A woman who is adult and Muslim, if she has been given an irrevocable divorce and is observing iddah, or her husband has died and she is observing iddah, during that time she must observe ihdād. Details of ihdād follow. In a hadith: I entered upon Umm Habiba, the wife of the Prophet... I heard the Messenger of Allah say: It is not permissible for a woman who believes in Allah and the Last Day to mourn for a deceased for more than three nights except for a husband for four months and ten days. From this hadith it is understood that the widow will mourn for four months ten days. And the woman given irrevocable divorce will mourn; this is deduced from the hadith because the word 'husband' (zawj) is used absolutely, whether he is the husband of an irrevocably divorced woman or the one who passed away. And from Umm Atiyya: The Prophet said: It is not permissible for a woman who believes in Allah and the Last Day to mourn for more than three except for a husband; then she should not apply kohl, nor wear dyed cloth except for 'asb cloth. In this hadith the word 'zawj' is general, so it includes both the deceased husband and the divorced one. Therefore a woman given irrevocable divorce will also mourn during her iddah. Just as a widow feels grief for her husband's death, so too the irrevocably divorced woman feels grief for being separated from her husband, so she also mourns. Imam al-Shafi'i says that the hadith restricts mourning to the widow only, so the irrevocably divorced woman does not need to mourn.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the obligation of ihdād (mourning) for a woman who has been given an irrevocable divorce (mubtūtah) versus a widow (mutawaffā 'anhā). The base text (matn) establishes that both types of women, provided they are adult and Muslim, must observe ihdād. The commentator then provides proof from two hadiths: the hadith of Umm Habiba and the hadith of Umm Atiyya, both stating that a woman believing in Allah and the Last Day cannot mourn for more than three days except for a husband, explicitly setting the mourning period for a husband as four months and ten days. The Hanafi position argues that the word 'husband' in these hadiths is general, applying to both a deceased husband and a husband who has divorced irrevocably. The commentator adds a rational point: just as a widow grieves for death, a divorced woman grieves for the loss of the marriage bond. Imam al-Shafi'i opposes this, holding that the hadith's exception (the word 'illa' for 'except') is restrictive, limiting mourning only to widows. The Hanafis respond that the general term 'zawj' includes all husbands. Thus, the Hanafi school rules that both must observe ihdād. According to the text, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that both categories of women must observe ihdād for four months and ten days.

Key Terms
إحداد (ihdād): Mourning by refraining from adornment and perfume for a specified period.
مبتوتة (mubtūtah): A woman given an irrevocable divorce (talāq bā'in) after which she cannot remarry the same husband without a new contract and consummation.
متوفى عنها زوجها (mutawaffā 'anhā zawjuhā): A woman whose husband has died and she is observing the widow's iddah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which women must observe ihdād (mourning)?
A) Only widows whose husbands have died
B) Only women given irrevocable divorce
C) Both widows and irrevocably divorced women provided they are adult and Muslim
D) All women in iddah regardless of cause

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school obligates ihdād on both widows and irrevocably divorced women based on the general term 'zawj' in the hadith and the analogy of grief.

Definition and General Prohibitions of Ihdād
Defines ihdād and lists prohibitions with exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of ihdād and identify the main prohibitions with their exception for a valid excuse.

Core Idea
Ihdād is refraining from perfume, adornment, oil, and kohl; only a valid excuse permits their use.

This lesson establishes the core meaning of ihdād (mourning period for a widow). The text defines it negatively — as abandoning certain beautification items. The Hanafi school lists specific items; the hadith underscores the prohibition of adornment, with a narrow exception for genuine necessity. Understanding this definition is the foundation for all rulings on widow mourning.

Guided Path
1
Define ihdād
State the definition from the text: leaving perfume, adornment (zīnah), oil, and kohl.
Note that the definition is 'abandon' (tark), not 'do' something.
2
List the prohibitions
Name the four categories: ṭīb (perfume), zīnah (adornment), duhn (oil), kuḥl (kohl). Adornment includes any item for beautification.
Examples: colourful clothes, jewelry, makeup.
3
Identify the exception
Note the exception: 'illa min ‘udhr' — except due to a valid excuse (e.g., illness, medical necessity).
The Urdu commentary explicitly mentions illness as an excuse.
4
Connect the hadith
Recognize that the prophetic hadith reinforces the prohibition, specifically mentioning kohl and dyed cloth, with a dispensation for wool ('asb).
This hadith is a key evidence (dalīl naqlī) for the ruling.

And ihdād is that she leaves perfume, adornment, oil, and kohl except due to an excuse. Mourning means to abandon perfume, adornment, oil, and kohl unless there is a valid excuse. For example, perfume, oil, kohl, etc. However, if there is a compulsion due to illness, she may use them. The hadith passed above. And in another hadith: from Salamah, the wife of the Prophet, that he said: The one whose husband has died... (footnote: The Prophet used to say: It is not permissible for a woman who believes in Allah and the Last Day to mourn for a dead person more than three days except for a husband for four months ten days.) He said: It is not permissible for a woman who believes in Allah and the Last Day to mourn more than three except for a husband, so she should not apply kohl, nor wear dyed cloth except for woolen cloth.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage provides a definition of ihdād (mourning) and lists the core prohibitions. The base text states: 'Ihdād is that she abandons perfume, adornment, oil, and kohl except due to an excuse.' The Urdu commentary explains that any items of beautification are included; examples are perfume, oil, kohl, etc. The exception is allowed for necessity, such as illness. The commentator then cites a hadith from Salamah (the wife of the Prophet) to reinforce the prohibition of these adornments. This hadith, also referenced earlier, emphasizes that the mourning woman should not use kohl or wear dyed cloth except for a specific type of woolen cloth ('asb). The definition is clear: ihdād is an act of leaving, not an act of doing. It is about refraining from beautification. The exception for excuse is important: if a woman needs to use something for a medical reason, she may do so without violating the obligation. The commentary does not elaborate on what constitutes a valid excuse, but the example of illness is given. This lesson is a memorization anchor for the essential prohibitions of ihdād.

Key Terms
إحداد (ihdād): Abstaining from perfume, adornment, oil, and kohl, except for a valid excuse.
الطيب (al-ṭīb): Perfume or any pleasant scent applied to the body or clothes.
الكحل (al-kuḥl): Kohl or eyeliner used for adornment.

Additional Prohibitions During Ihdād: Henna and Dyed Clothes
Expands ihdād prohibitions to henna and specific dyes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on using henna and wearing dyed clothes during ihdād, and identify the exception for necessity.

Core Idea
Henna and dyed clothes are adornment, prohibited in ihdād; necessity may permit them.

This passage extends the list of prohibitions during the mourning period (ihdād) to include henna application and garments dyed with safflower or saffron. The underlying principle is that any form of adornment is forbidden. However, the commentary, citing a hadith, introduces an exception: when there is a genuine need, such as removing an unpleasant smell, a limited use of perfume is allowed. This lesson unpacks the ruling and its evidence, helping you apply the prohibition and its exception correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited acts
Read the ruling carefully: it prohibits dyeing with henna (khitāb bil-ḥinnā) and wearing clothes dyed with safflower (usfur) or saffron (za'farān). Note that these are additions to the general prohibitions like kohl and perfume.
The Urdu commentary clarifies that these are forms of adornment (zīnat).
2
Understand the rationale
The commentary states that applying henna and wearing such dyed clothes are adornment. The purpose of ihdād is to avoid beautification out of respect for the deceased husband.
Adornment includes anything intended to enhance appearance, not just black clothes.
3
Recognize the evidential basis
The passage references a hadith from Umm Atiyyah (Abu Dawud no. 2304) which prohibits dyed clothes except a specific type, and also permits a little perfume after menses due to necessity (removing the unpleasant smell). This shows that the prohibition is strict but necessity can override it.
The hadith is not fully quoted here, but it is the primary dalil for the prohibition and the exception.
4
Apply the exception
The permission for perfume after menses indicates that necessity (ḍarūrah) can justify using adornment items. In the context of ihdād, if a woman has a genuine need (e.g., medical treatment), she may use henna or dyed clothes to the extent needed.
Necessity must be genuine and not merely for social occasions.
5
Distinguish from other prohibitions
Note that this ruling is an expansion of the earlier prohibitions. The same principle applies: any act of adornment is prohibited unless necessity allows it. The exception is narrow.
Other schools may have a different scope; the Hanafi school is cautious.

Ruling 2102 (24): And she should not dye with henna, nor wear a garment dyed with safflower or saffron. Dyeing, applying henna. And she should not apply henna nor wear clothes dyed with safflower or saffron. Applying henna, wearing clothes dyed with safflower or saffron is adornment, so during mourning she should not wear these. The hadith has passed above (Abu Dawud no. 2304). From Umm Atiyyah: ... He gave us permission at the time of purity (after menstruation) when one of us bathed from her menses to use a little scent of nubta of kust azfar. In this hadith, at the time of purity, permission is given to use a little perfume due to necessity. So it indicates that when necessary, it is permissible to use adornment items.

Commentary
This ruling expands on the prohibitions of ihdād by specifying two additional items: henna (khidāb) and clothes dyed with safflower ('usfur) or saffron (za'farān). The Urdu commentary clarifies that henna application and wearing such dyed clothes are forms of adornment and therefore prohibited during mourning. The commentator references the hadith of Umm Atiyyah (previously mentioned in Abu Dawud) which prohibits wearing dyed clothes except for a specific type of woolen cloth. Then, the commentary cites another part of the same hadith where the Prophet granted permission for a woman who has just become pure from menses to use a little perfume (a scent made from kust azfar) due to necessity (the unpleasant smell of menstrual blood). This shows that necessity can allow the use of adornment items. Thus, while henna and dyed clothes are generally forbidden, necessity may permit them. The ruling is clear: these are additional prohibitions beyond the general ones of kohl, perfume, etc.

عصفر (usfur): Safflower, a plant used for dyeing.
زعفران (za'farān): Saffron, a spice used for dyeing and perfuming.

Exemption from Ihdād for Non-Muslim and Minor Women
Exemption ruling with hadith proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for ihdād obligation and identify which categories of women are exempt, supporting with the hadith of Umm Atiyyah.

Core Idea
Ihdād is wajib only for adult Muslim women; non-Muslims and minors are not addressed.

This passage clarifies a key exception to the obligation of ihdād (mourning period). While ihdād is generally wajib for women in iddah, the Sharia only addresses those who are legally responsible – adult Muslim women. Non-Muslim women and minors are exempt because they are not mukallaf (legally accountable). The proof is drawn from the Prophet's hadith which explicitly restricts the command to 'a woman who believes in Allah and the Last Day', indicating the conditions of faith and adulthood.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Ihdād is wajib for a woman who is in iddah after divorce or death of husband, provided she is Muslim and adult.
This is the baseline obligation.
2
Locate the exemption in the text
The passage states: 'No ihdād upon a non-Muslim woman nor upon a minor.' This is a clear exception clause.
The Urdu commentary adds the rationale: non-Muslims are not addressed by Sharia, and minors are not responsible.
3
Understand the rationale
A non-Muslim woman is not mukallaf (obligated) for Islamic rulings due to lack of iman. A minor girl has not reached the age of taklif.
Thus, they are not commanded to perform ihdād.
4
Analyze the hadith proof
The hadith from Umm Atiyyah states: 'It is not permissible for a woman who believes in Allah and the Last Day to mourn for more than three...' The phrase 'who believes' excludes non-Muslims; 'woman' (imra'ah) in the context implies an adult, excluding minors.
This hadith is the dalil (proof) for the exemption.
5
Apply to real cases
If a non-Muslim woman divorces, she is not required to observe Islamic ihdād. Similarly, a girl under puberty who loses her husband is not obligated.
The exemption is unconditional; no additional requirements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۲۵) ولا احداد على كافرة ولا صغير
اور نہیں سوگ ہے کافرہ پر اور نہ بچی پر۔
كا فرہ عورت كفر کی وجہ سے شریعت کی مخاطب نہیں ہے۔ اور چھوٹی بچی بچی ہونے کی وجہ سے شریعت کی مخاطب نہیں ہے اس لئے ان دونوں پر سوگ نہیں ہے (۲) حدیث میں اس کا ثبوت ہے ۔ عن ام عطية قالت قال النبی الا الله لا يحل لامرأة تؤمن بالله واليوم الآخر ان تحد فوق ثلاث الخ اس حدیث میں لامرأۃ سے مراد بالغہ عورت ہے۔اور تؤمن بالله واليوم الآخر سے مومنہ عورت مراد ہے۔ اس لئے کافرہ عورت پر سوگ نہیں ہے۔
Ruling 2103 (25): And no mourning is required upon a non-Muslim woman nor upon a minor. And there is no mourning for a non-Muslim woman nor for a girl. The non-Muslim woman is not addressed by the Sharia because of her disbelief, and the minor girl is not addressed because of her minority, so neither of them must mourn. And proof of this is in the hadith: from Umm Atiyyah: The Prophet said: It is not permissible for a woman who believes in Allah and the Last Day to mourn for more than three... In this hadith, 'woman' (imra'ah) refers to an adult woman, and 'who believes in Allah and the Last Day' refers to a believing woman. Therefore, a non-Muslim woman does not have to mourn.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This ruling states that ihdād is not obligatory for a non-Muslim woman (kāfirah) or a minor girl (saghīrah). The Urdu commentary explains the reasoning: a non-Muslim woman is not addressed by the Sharia (ghayr mukhaṭabah) because she does not believe, and a minor is not legally responsible for religious obligations. The commentator then cites the hadith of Umm Atiyyah, which uses the phrase 'a woman who believes in Allah and the Last Day,' indicating that the obligation of ihdād is specifically for believing women. The word 'woman' is interpreted as an adult, excluding minors. Therefore, these two categories are exempt. This ruling is purely declaratory and is a memorization anchor for the conditions of obligation.

Key Terms
كافرة (kāfirah): A non-Muslim woman who does not believe in Islam.
صغير (ṣaghīr): A minor who has not reached puberty; not legally responsible.
احداد (ihdād): The period of mourning observed by a woman in iddah, which includes abstaining from adornment, perfume, and certain beautifications.
تکلیف (taklīf): Legal responsibility; the state of being mukallaf, which requires adulthood and sanity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Ihdād is only wajib for adult Muslim women; non-Muslims and minor girls are exempt because they are not addressed by the Sharia.

Slave Woman Must Observe Ihdād
Affirms slave woman's obligation for ihdād

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a slave woman must observe ihdād and explain why she is considered legally responsible.

Core Idea
Ihdād is wajib on every believing woman, free or slave.

This ruling clarifies that the obligation of ihdād (mourning period after divorce or death of husband) applies equally to slave women. Some might assume that a slave woman is not fully addressed by Islamic law, but the text asserts she is a believer and legally responsible (mukhaṭabah). Therefore, she must observe the same ihdād as a free woman.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its subject
What is being ruled upon? Ihdād (mourning). Who is the subject? A slave woman (amah).
The act is the same as for free women.
2
State the ruling
The ruling is that ihdād is wajib upon her. The Arabic 'على' indicates obligation.
3
Locate the rationale
Why is it wajib? The commentary explains: because she is a believer (mu'minah) and addressed by the Sharia (mukhaṭabah).
This shows that religious accountability (taklif) is based on faith, not freedom.
4
Compare with free woman
Is there any difference? No, both are equally obligated. The text does not reduce the duration or intensity.
This establishes equality of obligation.
5
Note the implication
This affirms that slave women have full religious capacity; their status does not exempt them from wajib acts of worship or 'iddah mourning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۲۶) وعلى الامة الاحداد
اور باندی پر سوگ ہے۔
باندی بھی مومنہ ہے اور مخاطبہ ہے اس لئے اس پر بھی سوگ ہے۔
Ruling 2104 (26): And upon the slave woman is ihdād. And upon the slave woman there is mourning. The slave woman is also a believer and is addressed (by the Sharia), so she must also mourn.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This short ruling clarifies that a female slave (amah) must observe ihdād just like a free woman. The commentary explains that she is a believer (mu'minah) and is mukhaṭabah (addressed by the Sharia), so she is obligated. No proof is given in this section, but the general proofs for ihdād apply to all female believers, free or slave. This ruling is a plain statement without any dispute and serves as a memorization anchor.

Key Terms
أمة (amah): A female slave who is a believer and legally responsible for religious duties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A slave woman is fully obligated to observe ihdād just like a free woman because she is a believing mukallafah.

No Ihdād in Fasid Marriage and Umm Walad's Iddah
Excludes ihdād for two specific iddah types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two exceptions to the obligation of ihdād and state the ruling for each.

Core Idea
Ihdād is not required in two specific iddahs: fasid marriage and umm walad.

In the previous lessons, we learned that ihdād (mourning) is generally required for a woman observing iddah after the death of her husband or after a final divorce. However, this ruling introduces two important exceptions: a woman in iddah due to a void marriage (nikāḥ fāsid) and an umm walad (slave concubine who has given birth to her master's child) do not observe ihdād. Understanding these exceptions is crucial for correctly applying the rules of iddah in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Recall that ihdād (abstaining from adornment and marriage) is wajib for a free woman observing iddah of death or iddah of a final divorce (talaq ba'in) from a valid marriage.
2
State the first exception
The text states: 'there is no ihdād in the iddah of a void (fasid) marriage.' A fasid marriage is one that is defective (e.g., marriage without witnesses). Note that the iddah itself is still required, but the mourning is not.
Why? Because the marriage is deficient from its beginning, so the grief is lessened.
3
State the second exception
The text also states: 'nor in the iddah of the umm walad.' An umm walad is a slave woman who has borne a child to her master. She observes iddah upon the master's death or manumission, but she does not observe ihdād.
Why? Because her status is not that of a free wife; the bond is ownership, not a free marriage.
4
Distinguish from other iddah cases
Be careful not to extend the exception. For a free woman from a valid marriage (even if the marriage ended by fasid? No, a valid marriage ends by death or divorce), ihdād is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۲۷) وليس فى عدة النكاح الفاسد ولا في عدة ام الولد احداد.
نكاح فاسد کی عدت میں اور ام ولد کی عدت میں سوگ نہیں ہے۔
Ruling 2105 (27): And there is no ihdād in the iddah of a void (fasid) marriage, nor in the iddah of the umm walad. In the iddah of a fasid marriage and in the iddah of the umm walad, there is no mourning.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This third ruling exempts two additional categories: (1) a woman observing iddah due to a void marriage (nikāḥ fāsid), and (2) an umm walad (a slave concubine who has given birth to her master's child) who must observe iddah after her master's death or manumission. No commentary is given in the text beyond repeating the ruling. The reason is that ihdād is linked to a valid marriage and the specific grief associated with termination of a valid marriage. In a fasid marriage, the marriage itself is deficient, and for an umm walad, the relationship is not that of a free wife, so ihdād is not required. This ruling is a memorization anchor.

Key Terms
نكاح فاسد (nikāḥ fāsid): A marriage that is defective due to missing a condition or presence of a prohibition, e.g., marriage without witnesses or a temporary marriage.
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave woman who has given birth to a child by her master, thereby gaining some rights and eventually freedom upon master's death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Iddah of a widow (free woman)
B) Iddah of a woman given irrevocable divorce
C) Iddah of a void marriage (nikāḥ fāsid)
D) Iddah of a free woman after divorce

Key Takeaway
Ihdād is not required in the iddah of a fasid marriage or an umm walad; these are two clear exceptions to the general rule.

Ihdad: Restrictions on Adornment and Exempted Women
Rulings on mourning restrictions and their application

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five restrictions of ihdad and identify which women are exempt from observing it.

Core Idea
Ihdad is wajib on believing adult free women; disbelievers and minors are exempt.

This passage from the Book of Iddah details the specific prohibitions a woman must observe during her mourning period. It also clarifies that not every woman is required to mourn—faith and legal capacity matter. We will work through the rulings, the hadith evidence, and the wisdom behind the exemptions.

Guided Path
1
List the prohibitions of ihdad
Identify all items a woman in iddah must avoid: henna, kohl, perfume, jewelry, and dyed clothes (safflower, saffron, ochre). Determine if there are exceptions for necessity.
The passage mentions 'she shall not dye with henna, nor wear a garment dyed with safflower or saffron.' The commentary expands to include all forms of adornment.
2
Identify who is exempt
Three exempt categories: (1) disbelieving woman, (2) minor girl, (3) woman in iddah of an invalid marriage (nikah fasid) or umm walad. Understand why each is exempt.
The hadith restricts the obligation to 'a woman who believes in Allah and the Last Day,' excluding disbelievers and minors who are not addressed.
3
Analyze the dalil
Examine the hadith from Umm Atiyyah: 'It is not lawful for a woman who believes in Allah and the Last Day to mourn more than three days except for a husband.' The phrase 'لا يحل' indicates prohibition, making ihdad wajib. The exemption clause proves its obligation for husbands.
The word 'تؤمن' restricts the obligation to believing women.
4
Apply to slave women
The text explicitly includes slave women. Argue why a slave woman is obligated even though she is not free: she is a believing adult, thus addressed by the command.
This illustrates that legal capacity (taklif) is based on faith and adulthood, not freedom.

Book of Iddah: (24) She shall not dye with henna, nor wear a garment dyed with safflower or saffron. (25) There is no mourning upon a disbelieving woman nor a minor. (26) And mourning is upon a slave woman. (27) And there is no mourning in the iddah of an invalid marriage nor in the iddah of an umm walad. She does not wear garments dyed with safflower or ochre, nor jewelry, nor does she dye (with henna), nor apply kohl. (Hadith from Abu Dawud) This hadith shows that a woman in iddah cannot wear safflower-dyed or ochre-dyed clothes, jewelry, henna, or kohl. However, in necessity she may use these things; the proof is the hadith from Umm Atiyyah: '...and he permitted us, at the time of purity, when one of us bathed from her menses, to use a little bit of costus and azfar.' This hadith allows using a little perfume when necessary, indicating that using adornments is permissible in necessity. (24) And she should not apply henna nor wear a garment dyed with safflower or saffron. Applying henna and wearing safflower or saffron-dyed clothes is adornment, so she should not wear them during mourning. The hadith has passed (Abu Dawud no. 2304). (25) There is no mourning upon a disbelieving woman nor a minor. The disbelieving woman is not addressed by the Shariah due to disbelief, and the minor not due to being a minor, so there is no mourning for them. The hadith proves this: Umm Atiyyah said: 'The Prophet said: It is not lawful for a woman who believes in Allah and the Last Day to mourn more than three days...' In this hadith, 'a woman' means an adult woman, and 'believes in Allah and the Last Day' means a believing woman, so a disbelieving woman has no mourning. (26) And mourning is upon a slave woman. The slave woman is also believing and addressed, so she also mourns. (27) There is no mourning in the iddah of an invalid marriage nor in the iddah of an umm walad. In invalid marriage, the marriage is separated, so it is good that it ended, thus no sorrow. Similarly, the umm walad's master is not her husband; it is good that she is freed, hence no mourning. This ruling is based on the principle: one who is not a husband does not have mourning.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the rulings on ihdad (mourning) during iddah. The author first lists the restrictions: a woman in iddah must avoid henna, and clothes dyed with safflower (wars) or saffron (za'faran). The commentary clarifies that this includes any dyed cloth (like with 'usfur or ochre), jewelry, henna, and kohl. Then, it addresses who must observe ihdad: it is not required of a disbelieving woman (because she is not addressed by Islamic law) or a minor girl. However, a slave woman (even if not free) must observe ihdad because she is a believer and addressed. Finally, there is no ihdad in the iddah of an invalid marriage (nikah fasid) or of an umm walad (a slave woman who bore her master's child), because in these cases the separation is not a cause of sorrow. The commentary provides hadith evidence, including the well-known hadith from Umm Atiyyah prohibiting mourning beyond three days for any woman believing in Allah and the Last Day. This passage teaches that ihdad is an act of worship that requires faith and legal capacity.

Key Terms
الإحداد (al-ihdad): Abstaining from adornment (beauty) during the iddah of death or irrevocable divorce; includes avoiding henna, kohl, dyed clothes, jewelry, perfume.
عدة (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of her husband before remarrying.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Ihdad is wajib only on believing adult women in iddah of a valid marriage; exemptions apply based on faith and legal capacity.

Staying in the Home During Iddah: Rulings for Divorcées and Widows
Ruling with proof on leaving the home during iddah for divorced and widowed women.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the iddah residence rules for divorced women (raj'iyyah and bainunah) versus widows, and apply the Hanafi position with the Quranic proof.

Core Idea
A divorced woman must stay home day and night during iddah; a widow may go out by day for necessity but must sleep at home.

This passage from al-Sharh al-Sameri clarifies a key fiqh ruling: the obligation to remain in the marital home during iddah. While the Quranic verse (65:1) provides a general prohibition against leaving, the Hanafi school distinguishes between divorced women and widows based on necessity and maintenance. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the iddah requirements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of women in iddah
List the three types: revocably divorced (mutallaqah raj'iyyah), irrevocably divorced (mutallaqah bainunah), and widow (mutawaffa 'anha zawjuha). Note the different rulings that apply.
2
State the ruling for each category regarding leaving the home
For divorced women (both raj'iyyah and bainunah): not permissible to leave day or night. For widows: permissible to leave during the day and part of the night for necessity, but must sleep at home.
3
Understand the exception for widows
The widow's dispensation is based on the need to earn a livelihood, as she may not have maintenance (nafaqah) during her iddah. She may go out for work or essential errands during daylight, but must return home to sleep.
The Hanafi school restricts night outings strictly to necessity.
4
Locate the Quranic proof
The primary dalil is Surah al-Talaq (65:1): 'Do not drive them out of their houses, nor shall they leave except if they commit a clear indecency.' The verse commands both husbands and wives to maintain the residence.
The verse is qat'i in transmission and indicates a clear prohibition.
5
Explain the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative 'la tukhrijūhunna wa lā yakhrujna' constitutes a categorical prohibition. The exception 'illā an ya'tīna bi fāḥishah mubayyinah' is interpreted narrowly by the Hanafi school, not extending to general necessities.
This yields the ruling that divorced women cannot leave at all; widows' permission is derived from other considerations (need, not the exception).

Source Text

نکاح فاسد کی وجہ سے تفریق ہوئی ہو جس کی عدت گزار رہی ہو تو اس میں سوگ نہیں ہے۔

(29) It is not permissible for a revocably divorced woman (raj'iyyah) and an irrevocably divorced woman (bainunah) to leave her house at night or during the day. And the woman whose husband has died may go out during the day and part of the night, but she should not spend the night other than in her house. The commentary says: All women in iddah must stay in the house where the divorce or death occurred. However, a widow may go out during the day for earning sustenance, and can also be out for part of the night, but she must sleep in that house. The evidence is the verse: 'O Prophet, when you divorce women, divorce them for their waiting period and keep count of the waiting period, and fear Allah, your Lord.'
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on leaving the marital home during iddah. For a woman divorced revocably (raj'iyyah) or irrevocably (bainunah), it is not permissible to leave the house either night or day. However, a widow may leave during the day for her livelihood and can be out for part of the night, but she must return to sleep at the house. The basis for this is the Qur'anic instruction to the Prophet (peace be upon him) regarding divorce, which implies staying in the home. The difference between the divorced and the widow is based on need: the widow often requires work to support herself, whereas the divorced woman still has maintenance (nafaqah) during iddah. The Hanafi school emphasizes that the home is the place where the iddah is observed; leaving without a valid excuse can affect the validity of the iddah.

Key Terms
مطلقة رجعية (mutallaqah raj'iyyah): A woman divorced with one or two revocable divorces; her husband can take her back without a new marriage contract during iddah.
مطلقة بائنة (mutallaqah bainunah): A woman divorced irrevocably (e.g., third divorce or khul'); reconciliation requires a new marriage.
متوفى عنها زوجها (mutawaffa 'anha zawjuha): A woman whose husband has died.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Leaving the house at night to sleep at a friend's house
B) Going out during the day to buy groceries
C) Leaving for a week-long visit to her parents
D) Going out at midnight for a walk

Key Takeaway
The iddah residence is a religious obligation; divorced women must stay home day and night, while widows may leave during the day for necessities but must sleep at home.

Ruling on Confinement in the Home During 'Iddah
States the confinement rules for divorced and widowed women in 'iddah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on confinement for divorced women and widows during 'iddah, and identify the Quranic and hadith proofs for each category.

Core Idea
A divorced woman must stay home day and night; a widow may leave by day but must return at night.

The period of 'iddah carries specific residence obligations. This lesson clarifies the Hanafi position on where a woman must stay during her waiting period, distinguishing between divorced women and widows. You will examine the primary proofs from the Quran, hadith, and companion practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories
Recognize the three types of women mentioned: revocably divorced (raj'iyyah), irrevocably divorced (ba'inah), and widow (mutawaffa 'anha zawjuha). Note the different rulings for each.
The ruling for divorced women is stricter than for widows.
2
State the ruling for divorced women
From the matn: 'It is not permissible for the revocably divorced and the irrevocably divorced woman to leave her house night or day.' Understand that this is a complete prohibition.
No exception is mentioned for divorced women in this passage.
3
State the ruling for widows
The matn says: 'The one whose husband has died goes out during the day and part of the night, and does not spend the night in other than her home.' This permits daytime leaving for necessity but requires nighttime return.
‘Part of the night’ allows errands extending slightly after dark.
4
Examine the first proof: Quran 65:1 and 2:240
Quran 65:1 prohibits expulsion and leaving except for fahishah. Quran 2:240 originally bequeathed a year's maintenance and residence to widows, but was abrogated in duration; it still implies permission to leave ('if they go out, no sin upon you').
Hanafi interpretation: 2:240 allows daytime exit for widows.
5
Examine the second proof: Hadith of Zaynab and athar of Ibn 'Umar
The hadith of Zaynab bint Ka'b shows the Prophet ordering a widow to stay in her house even when she had no maintenance. The athar of Ibn 'Umar emphasizes that the widow and irrevocably divorced woman should not spend the night outside her home.
These proofs reinforce the rule, especially for widows.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يجوز للمطلقة الرجعية والمبتوتة الخروج من بيتها ليلا او نهارا والمتوفى عنها زوجها تخرج نهارا وبعض الليل ولا تبيت في غير منزلها. لا تخرجوهن من بيوتهن ولا يخرجن الا ان يأتين بفاحشة مبينة. والذين يتوفون منكم ويذرون ازواجا وصية لازواجهم متاعا الى الحول غير اخراج فان خرجن فلا جناح عليكم في ما فعلن في انفسهن من معروف. عن عمته زينب بنت كعب بن عجرة اخبرتها انها جاءت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم تسأله ان ترجع الى اهلها في بني حذرة وان زوجها خرج في طلب اعبد له ابقوا حتى اذا كان بطرف القدوم لحقهم فقتلوه قالت فسألت رسول الله ان ارجع الى اهلي فان زوجي لم يترك لى مسكنا يملكه ولا نفقة قالت فقال رسول الله نعم قالت فانصرفت حتى اذا كنت في الحجرة او في المسجد ناداني رسول الله او امر بي فنوديت له فقال كيف قلت قالت فرددت عليه القصة التي ذكرت له من شان زوجي قال امكثى فى بيتك حتى يبلغ الكتب اجله. عن عبد الله بن عمر قال لا تبيت المتوفى عنها زوجها ولا المبتوتة الا في بيتها.
It is not permissible for the revocably divorced and the irrevocably divorced woman to leave her house night or day. And the one whose husband has died goes out during the day and part of the night, and does not spend the night in other than her home. Do not drive them out of their houses, nor shall they go out, unless they commit a clear immorality. And those of you who die and leave wives, a bequest for their wives, provision for a year, without expulsion. But if they leave, there is no sin upon you for what they do with themselves according to custom. From his paternal aunt Zaynab bint Ka'b ibn 'Ujrah: She came to the Messenger of Allah asking to return to her family in Banu Hudhrah, and her husband had gone out in search of runaway slaves until at the edge of al-Qadum they caught up and killed him. She asked to return to her family, as her husband left no owned dwelling nor maintenance. The Prophet said 'Yes.' She turned away until in the room or the mosque, the Prophet called her and asked 'What did you say?' She repeated the story, and he said 'Stay in your house until the term reaches its end.' From 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar: The widow and the irrevocably divorced woman should not spend the night except in her house.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage details the confinement rules for women in 'iddah according to the Hanafi school. The matn distinguishes three categories: (1) a woman divorced with revocable divorce (raj'iyyah) and (2) with irrevocable divorce (ba'inah) are not permitted to leave their homes at any time, day or night. (3) A widow may go out during the day and part of the night for necessary errands but must return to sleep in her designated home. The proofs are drawn from Quran 65:1 (general injunction not to expel women), Quran 2:240 (bequest for widows to stay a year, with permission to leave), the hadith of Zaynab bint Ka'b (where the Prophet ordered her to remain in her house even without maintenance), and the athar of Ibn 'Umar (emphasizing no overnight stay elsewhere). The ruling is strict for divorced women to avoid temptation and confusion of lineage, while widows are granted more leniency due to economic needs. The commentary notes that the house meant is the matrimonial home where the divorce or death occurred, and necessity (e.g., earning a living) allows widows to leave during daylight.

Key Terms
رجعة (raj'ah): Revocable divorce; the husband can take back his wife within 'iddah without a new contract.
بتة (battah): Irrevocable divorce (talaq ba'in); the marriage ends and cannot be resumed except through a new marriage.
متوفى عنها زوجها (mutawaffa 'anha zawjuha): Widow; a woman whose husband has died.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a widow going out during the night?
A) She may go out at any time
B) She may go out during the night but must return at dawn
C) She may not spend the night outside her home
D) She is prohibited from going out at all

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school strictly confines divorced women to the home but grants widows daytime mobility due to economic needs, as supported by Quran and hadith.

Obligation of 'Iddah in the Marital Home
Obligation to observe 'iddah in the marital home

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the place of 'iddah and identify the specific residence required.

Core Idea
The mu'taddah must stay in the house she lived in at the time of separation — moving without necessity breaks the law.

This short passage from the matn of Al-Sharh al-Samiri establishes a fundamental rule for a woman observing 'iddah: she must remain in the same dwelling she occupied when the marital relationship ended. This ruling applies to both divorce and widowhood, as per the Hanafi school. While seemingly simple, this rule carries important legal consequences and reflects the school's commitment to preserving the woman's stability and preventing suspicion during the waiting period.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Obligation
Recognize that the text imposes a duty (wajib) on the mu'taddah: she must observe 'iddah. The verb 'an ta'tadda' indicates an action that is legally prescribed.
The obligation is for the entire 'iddah period, not just part of it.
2
Specify the House
Determine which house is intended: 'the house that is attributed to her by residence at the time of separation occurring.' This means the house she was living in when the divorce or death occurred, regardless of ownership.
If she had been temporarily away (e.g., visiting family), the operative reference is her usual marital home.
3
Understand the Legal Effect
The ruling requires continuous residence in that house during the 'iddah. Leaving without a valid excuse (e.g., fear of harm, expulsion by heirs) would violate the obligation and may affect her maintenance rights.
The Hanafi school allows exceptions only for dire necessity, such as threat to life or the house being unsafe.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى المعتدة ان تعتد في منزل الذي يضاف اليها بالسكنى حال وقوع الفرقة
And upon the woman in 'iddah is to observe 'iddah in the house that is attributed to her by residence at the time of the separation occurring.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the matn. It establishes the general principle that a woman in 'iddah (whether due to divorce or death) must remain in the house she was living in when the separation occurred. The word 'yudafu ilayha' indicates the house is assigned to her as her dwelling. This applies to both revocable and irrevocable divorce, as well as widowhood, as per the Hanafi school. The ruling is based on the Quranic injunction 'la tukhrijuhunna min buyutihinna' (Surah al-Talaq 65:1) and the hadith 'umkuthi fi baytiki hatta yablugha al-kitabu ajalahu' (stay in your house until the prescribed period reaches its term). However, the text does not quote these proofs explicitly; it is a concise legal statement meant for memorization.

Key Terms
المعتدة (al-mu'taddah): A woman who is observing the 'iddah period after divorce or death of her husband.
عدة (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or widowhood before she can remarry.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) In her father's house.
B) In the house she lived in at the time of separation.
C) In any house provided by her ex-husband.
D) In a mosque or public shelter.

Key Takeaway
The mu'taddah must remain in the residence she occupied at the time of separation, unless a valid necessity permits relocation.

Moving from the 'Iddah Home Due to Necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when a mu'taddah may leave her 'iddah home and evaluate the proofs that establish the exception for necessity.

Core Idea
A woman in 'iddah may move only if forced by necessity, like insufficient housing or danger.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on an exception to the general rule that the mu'taddah must remain in her husband's home during 'iddah. The source text presents several proofs from the Sunnah and the actions of the Companions that allow moving when there is genuine need. Understanding these proofs helps distinguish between a general permission and a restricted exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Find the main ruling in the text: the mu'taddah may move if her share of the house is insufficient and the heirs expel her. This is a conditional permission based on necessity.
The ruling is mubah, but only when the cause is present.
2
Examine the Proofs
The text cites four proofs: 1) Hadith of Jabir – Prophet allows a divorced woman to go out for need. 2) Athar of Ibn Umar – daytime outings permitted, but must return at night. 3) Aisha's criticism of Fatimah bint Qais' hadith – restricts permission to unsafe location. 4) Ali moving his daughter – confirms moving for safety.
Each proof shows that necessity overrides the general prohibition of leaving the home.
3
Analyze the Usul (Methodology)
The Hanafi school uses these multiple proofs to derive a nuanced ruling. They interpret the general command to stay as established by the Qur'an (Surah 65:1), and then treat the exceptions as specific cases (takhsis) based on need.
The principle is: 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' – rulings depend on their causes.
4
Apply the Thamarah (Practical Outcome)
The practical consequence: a widow who is expelled by heirs may move; a divorced woman may go out for work if necessary; but staying at night remains obligatory unless danger exists. This distinguishes Hanafi fatwa from stricter schools.
The mufta bihi is that moving is permissible with a valid excuse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فان كان نصيبها من دار الميت لا يكفيها واخرجها الورثة من نصيبهم انتقلت. عن جابر قال: طُلّقت خالتي فأرادت أن تجد نخلها فزجرها رجل أن تخرج فأتت النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال: بلى فجدي نخلَك فإنك عسى أن تصدَّقي أو تفعلي معروفا. عن ابن عمر قال: المطلقة والمتوفى عنها زوجها تخرجان بالنهار ولا تبيتان ليلة تامة غير بيوتهما. لقد عابت ذلك عائشة أشد العيب حديث فاطمة بنت قيس وقالت: إن فاطمة كانت في مكان وحش فخيف على ناحيتها فلذلك رخص لها رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم. قال: نقل علي أم كلثوم بعد قتل عمر بسبع ليال وقال: لأنها كانت في دار الإمارة.
If her share from the deceased's house is insufficient for her and the heirs expel her from their share, she may move. From Jabir: My maternal aunt was divorced and wanted to cut her palm dates, so a man scolded her for going out. She came to the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, and he said: Yes, cut your dates, for you may give charity or do a good deed. From Ibn Umar: The divorced woman and the widow go out during the day but do not stay overnight completely away from their houses. Aisha severely criticized the hadith of Fatimah bint Qais, saying: Fatimah was in a desolate place and her safety was feared, so the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, allowed her. He (Ali) said: Ali moved Umm Kulthum seven nights after Umar's murder and said: Because she was in the governor's residence.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage provides proofs for the exception to the general rule. The main ruling is that if the mu'taddah's share of the deceased husband's house is too small to live in and the heirs expel her, she may move to another place. This is a necessity. The hadith of Jabir shows the Prophet allowing a divorced woman to go out to harvest dates, which is a need. The athar of Ibn Umar states that divorced and widowed women may go out during the day but must return at night. Aisha's criticism of Fatimah bint Qais's hadith indicates that the permission to move was due to the dangerous location, not a general license. Finally, Ali moved his daughter Umm Kulthum from the governor's palace to a safer place after Umar's death, confirming that necessity permits relocation. The Hanafi school uses these proofs to allow moving only when there is genuine need, such as fear for safety or expulsion by heirs.

Key Terms
انتقلت (intaqalat): She moves from the original 'iddah home to another place due to necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) She may never move under any circumstance.
D) Only if she remarries.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits a mu'taddah to move from her 'iddah home only when necessity demands, such as insufficient housing or safety concerns, based on multiple Sunna proofs.

Traveling with a Revocably Divorced Wife
Prohibits the husband from traveling with his revocably divorced wife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a husband traveling with his revocably divorced wife and explain the proof from the practice of Ibn Umar.

Core Idea
A husband may not travel with his revocably divorced wife to prevent unintentional return (raj'a) during the 'iddah.

This passage addresses a specific case in Talaq: when a husband revocably divorces his wife, can he travel with her? The text gives a clear ruling—it is not permissible—and supports it with the cautious practice of Ibn Umar. This lesson unpacks the reasoning, the legal classification, and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is the husband traveling with his revocably divorced wife. The ruling stated is 'not permissible' (la yajuz).
The ruling is absolute in the matn.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites the athar of Ibn Umar: when he issued a revocable divorce, he would not enter her room without permission. Al-Sha'bi adds that the companions said he would tap his sandals (as a signal).
This is a Companion practice (qawl al-sahabi) and a form of Sunnah.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Ibn Umar's extreme caution about entering her room shows that even that proximity is restricted. Traveling together involves far more proximity and privacy, so it is even more prohibited by analogy (qiyas).
The analogy is from a lesser to a greater prohibition.
4
Note the Hanafi hukm level
The ruling is classified as makruh tahrimi (prohibited due to a probable harm), not haram, because the dalil is zanni (a Companion practice).
The school uses sadd al-dhara'i (blocking pretexts) as the underlying rationale.
5
Identify the 'illah (effective cause)
The effective cause is the potential for unintentional return (raj'a) without proper intention, which could harm the wife's rights and complicate the 'iddah.
This 'illah is the basis for the prohibition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يجوز ان يسافر الزوج بالمطلقة الرجعية. عن ابن عمر انه كان اذا طلق طلاقا يملك الرجعة لم يدخل حتى يستأذن وقال الشعبي كان اصحابنا يقولون يخفق بنعليه.
And it is not permissible for the husband to travel with the revocably divorced woman. From Ibn Umar: When he gave a divorce that allowed return (raj'a), he would not enter (her room) until he sought permission. And al-Sha'bi said: Our companions used to say: He would tap his sandals.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof. The husband is prohibited from traveling with his revocably divorced wife (muttalaqa raj'iyya). The reason is that during travel, physical proximity may lead to an unintentional return (raj'a) to the marriage, which would then require a fresh divorce, extending the 'iddah. The athar of Ibn Umar shows that even entering her room requires permission, so traveling together is more severely prohibited. The Hanafi school considers this makruh tahrimi, as it could cause confusion. However, if he does travel, it is still valid because she remains his wife during the 'iddah, but he must avoid actions that imply return.

Key Terms
المطلقة الرجعية (al-muttalaqa al-raj'iyya): A woman who has been divorced with a revocable divorce, meaning the husband can take her back without a new marriage contract during the 'iddah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because she is no longer his wife.
B) Because it may lead to unintentional return (raj'a) and complicate the 'iddah.
C) Because she must stay in one place for the 'iddah.
D) Because traveling is haram for women.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits traveling with a revocably divorced wife to block the means to unintentional raj'a, even though the marriage is still valid.

The Irrevocably Divorced Wife Remarried in Her ʿIddah
Ruling on a second marriage during ʿiddah and subsequent divorce before consummation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the majority Hanafi ruling on mahr and ʿiddah when a bain divorcee is remarried in her ʿiddah and then divorced before consummation, and explain the dissenting opinion of Imam Muhammad.

Core Idea
Remarrying during ʿiddah interrupts the first ʿiddah and triggers a new one, even without consummation, by legal fiction.

This lesson examines a specific scenario: a man divorces his wife irrevocably, then remarries her during her ʿiddah, and then divorces her again before any consummation. The classical Hanafi texts present an internal disagreement on the consequences for mahr and ʿiddah. By understanding this dispute, you will grasp a key principle of Hanafi jurisprudence: the legal fiction (istiʿārat al-waṭʾ) that transfers the effect of prior intimacy to the new marriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the act being ruled upon
The scenario is: a man divorces his wife with a irrevocable divorce (talaq bain), then remarries her during her ʿiddah, and then divorces her again before consummation. The rulings concern the mahr (dower) and the ʿiddah (waiting period).
The marriage during ʿiddah is considered valid in Hanafi school.
2
State the majority Hanafi position (Abū Ḥanīfa and Abū Yūsuf)
According to the majority, the husband must pay full mahr and the wife must observe a new, complete ʿiddah. This is the ruling given in the matn (source text).
The majority view is the muftā bihī (operative opinion).
3
Identify the dissenting position of Imam Muḥammad
Imam Muḥammad holds that the wife receives only half the mahr and she completes the original ʿiddah (the one from the first divorce).
This is an intra-Ḥanafī ikhtilāf.
4
Analyze the reasoning behind each view
The majority (Abū Ḥanīfa and Abū Yūsuf) use a legal fiction (istiʿārat al-waṭʾ): the prior intimacy from the first marriage is considered to have occurred in the second marriage, so full mahr and a new ʿiddah are due. Imam Muḥammad argues that since there was no actual consummation in the new marriage, only half mahr is needed and the first ʿiddah continues.
This difference stems from how they treat the causal link between marriage and the obligations of mahr and ʿiddah.
5
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilāf)
In practice, under the majority view, the wife gets full mahr and starts a new 3-month or 3-cycle ʿiddah. Under Muḥammad's view, she only gets half mahr and completes the original ʿiddah.
This affects financial and timing obligations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واذا طلق الرجل امرأته طلاقا بائنا ثم تزوجها في عدتها وطلقها قبل ان يدخل بها فعلیه مهر کامل و عليها عدة مستقبلة وقال محمد رحمه الله لها نصف المهر وعليها العدة الأولى.
And if a man divorces his wife with an irrevocable divorce, then marries her in her 'iddah and divorces her before consummating with her, then upon him is a full mahr and upon her is a new 'iddah. And Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) said: For her is half the mahr and upon her is the first 'iddah.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute. The scenario: A man gives his wife an irrevocable divorce (talaq bain). During her 'iddah, he marries her again (which is permissible because she is still in his 'iddah). Then before consummating this new marriage, he divorces her. The ruling of Imam Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: The husband must pay full mahr, and the woman must observe a new, complete 'iddah (different from the first). The reasoning is that the prior marital relations from the first marriage are considered as having occurred in the second marriage as well (by legal fiction), so the second divorce after marriage triggers full mahr and a new 'iddah. Imam Muhammad disagrees: She only gets half mahr (since there was no consummation) and she must complete the first 'iddah (the one from the first divorce). The difference stems from whether the first marriage's intimacy can be 'transferred' to the second marriage. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, as the Urdu commentary indicates they are the majority. The athar from al-Sha'bi is referenced but not fully quoted in the Arabic text.

Key Terms
طلاق بائن (talaq bain): An irrevocable divorce that ends the marriage and removes the husband's right of return during ʿiddah.
عدة مستقبلة (iddah mustaqbilah): A new, complete ʿiddah that begins from the second divorce, independent of the first.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Half mahr and continue the first ʿiddah.
B) Full mahr and new ʿiddah.
C) Full mahr and continue the first ʿiddah.
D) No mahr and no ʿiddah.

Key Takeaway
The majority Hanafi opinion rules that remarrying in ʿiddah triggers a new ʿiddah and full mahr, even without consummation, by legally transferring the effect of prior intimacy.

Husband Traveling with Revocably Divorced Wife
Ruling on husband traveling with revocably divorced wife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a husband traveling with his revocably divorced wife and explain the reasoning based on the risk of unintentional return.

Core Idea
Traveling with a revocably divorced wife is makruh tahrimi due to risk of unintended return, but if done, it is valid.

In this passage, the author addresses a specific scenario during the iddah period: the husband's travel with his revocably divorced wife (muta·laqah jaj‘iyyah). The text establishes a ruling that balances the prohibition rooted in potential harm to the iddah with the permissibility because the marriage still subsists. We will unpack the ruling, its reasoning, and the evidence from early authorities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Focus on the verbal phrase 'la yajuz' (not permissible). The act is the husband traveling with his revocably divorced wife during her iddah. The ruling is that it is prohibited (makruh tahrimi).
The term 'la yajuz' often indicates prohibition, but the subsequent permissibility clause shows it is not absolute haram.
2
Understand the reasoning (illah)
The text gives the reason: traveling together may lead to intimacy, resulting in an unintended return (raj‘ah). This prolongs the iddah if divorce is repeated. The safeguard against this harm is the prohibition.
The illah is the risk of involuntary return, not the travel itself.
3
Note the exception clause
The text states, 'if he does travel, it is permissible because she is still his wife.' This indicates that the prohibition is not invalidating but rather a discouragement. The marriage bond remains intact during iddah.
This distinguishes makruh tahrimi from haram: the act is valid but sinful.
4
Connect to the evidence (athar)
Although not directly quoted in the matn, the commentary mentions an athar from Ibn ‘Umar that he would not enter upon his revocably divorced wife without permission, and from Sha‘bi that the Companions would make noise before entering. This implies maintaining distance, supporting the ruling.
The evidence reinforces the idea of avoiding close contact during iddah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۱۱۰] (۳۲) ولا يجوز ان يسافر الزوج بالمطلقة الرجعية ... اور نہیں جائز ہے کہ شوہر سفر کرے مطلقہ رجعیہ کے ساتھ۔ مطلقہ رجعیہ کے ساتھ سفر کرے گا تو ممکن ہے کہ بے اختیاری طور پر رجعت ہو جائے حالانکہ وہ رجعت کرنا نہیں چاہتا تھا۔ اس کے بعد پھر طلاق دے گا اور عدت لمبی ہو جائے گی اس لئے مطلقہ رجعیہ کے ساتھ شوہر سفر نہ کرے ... اور اگر سفر کر ہی لیا تو جائز ہے کیونکہ وہ ابھی تک اس کی بیوی ہے۔ البتہ زیادہ قربت کرنے سے رجعت ہو جائے گی۔
It is not permissible for the husband to travel with the revocably divorced woman. If he travels with a revocably divorced woman, it is possible that a return might occur involuntarily even though he did not intend to return. Then afterwards he would divorce her and the iddah would become prolonged. Therefore, the husband should not travel with the revocably divorced woman... And if he does travel, it is permissible because she is still his wife. However, from excessive intimacy, a return will occur.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement regarding the husband's travel with his revocably divorced wife. The author states that it is not permissible (la yajuz) for the husband to take his revocably divorced wife on a journey. The reasoning is that traveling together may lead to intimacy, which would constitute a return (raj'ah) unintentionally, and then if he divorces again, the iddah would be prolonged. Nevertheless, if he does travel with her, it is permissible because she remains his wife during the iddah. The warning is that excessive closeness will effect a return. The author cites an athar from Ibn 'Umar that he would not enter upon his revocably divorced wife without permission, and from Sha'bi that the Companions would make noise with sandals before entering. This supports the idea of maintaining distance.

Key Terms
مطلقہ رجعیہ (mutallaqah raj'iyyah): A woman who has been given a revocable divorce (first or second talaq) and is in her iddah period, during which her husband may return to her without a new marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband travels with his revocably divorced wife without intending to return, what is the ruling?
A) It is absolutely forbidden and invalid.
B) It is disliked (makruh tahrimi) but if done, it is permissible.
C) It is completely allowed.
D) It is invalid and the divorce is nullified.

Key Takeaway
Travel with a revocably divorced wife is makruh tahrimi due to the risk of unintended return, but valid if done; intimacy during such travel effects an automatic return.

Talaq — Divorce
